The primary function of this cell is to oversee the daily reporting of seizures, which refers to the confiscation of illicit cash, goods, or materials intended for influencing voters, and to facilitate coordination among the different enforcement agencies involved in maintaining the integrity of the election.

At the state level, within the office of the Chief Electoral Officer, another senior Nodal Officer is appointed to manage broader coordination efforts. This official must hold a rank equivalent to or higher than a Joint Chief Electoral Officer. The scope of responsibility for this state-level officer is extensive, encompassing the synchronization of expenditure monitoring activities with the central Election Commission, the organization of training programs for personnel involved in monitoring, and the education of political party functionaries regarding financial regulations. This officer acts as the central hub connecting all District Election Officers, other state-level Nodal Officers, independent Expenditure Observers, and the full spectrum of law enforcement agencies. Their role ensures that information flows seamlessly between local operations and the highest levels of the election administration, creating a unified front against financial malpractice.

Parallel to the administrative structure, the police department establishes its own dedicated leadership for election expenditure monitoring. An officer holding the rank of Inspector General at the State Police Headquarters is designated as the Nodal Officer for police-related coordination. This high-ranking official is tasked with liaising with Flying Squads and Static Surveillance Teams, which are mobile and stationary units respectively deployed to intercept illegal activities. To ensure effective communication, the contact details of this Inspector General, including telephone, fax, and mobile numbers, are distributed to Expenditure Observers, the Investigation Directorate, the Excise Department, and other relevant law enforcement bodies prior to the election. A critical duty of this officer is to guarantee that all personnel assigned to Flying Squads and Static Surveillance Teams receive adequate training on their roles and legal powers. Furthermore, this officer is responsible for compiling seizure reports submitted by district Superintendents of Police from these field units. These reports are aggregated into a composite Daily Activity Report, which must be transmitted every day to the Expenditure Monitoring Cell of the Election Commission in a specific prescribed format, with a duplicate copy sent to the Chief Electoral Officer. On the actual day of polling, the urgency and importance of this data peak; the Inspector General must submit a comprehensive final report detailing all seizures made by Flying Squads, Static Surveillance Teams, and police authorities throughout the entire election period, from the initial announcement date up to the polling day. This final compilation must reach the Chief Electoral Officer before one o'clock in the afternoon on polling day, with a simultaneous copy forwarded to the Election Commission. These reports utilize specific standardized forms, often referenced as Annexure-B8, B9, and B10 for daily activities and Annexure-C4 for the final poll-day report, ensuring uniformity in data collection across the nation.

Beyond the core election machinery and police force, the Election Commission leverages a wide array of other enforcement agencies to strengthen the monitoring net. Upon request from the Commission, diverse organizations such as the Central Board of Indirect Taxes and Customs, the Enforcement Directorate, the Directorate of Revenue Intelligence, Central Goods and Services Tax authorities, the Financial Intelligence Unit of India, the Reserve Bank of India, State Lotteries Boards, the Department of Post, the Railway Protection Force, the Indian Coast Guard, and various paramilitary forces including the Central Industrial Security Force, Border Security Force, Sashastra Seema Bal, Indo-Tibetan Border Police, and Assam Rifles are required to appoint their own Nodal Officers. Additionally, state-level departments like Commercial Tax, State Goods and Services Tax, and Forest Departments also designate liaison officers. The purpose of involving this broad coalition is to facilitate intelligence sharing and coordinate the interception and seizure of prohibited items such as unaccounted cash, liquor, drugs and narcotics, precious metals, and freebies distributed to sway voters. This multi-agency approach recognizes that financial irregularities in elections often intersect with customs violations, money laundering, tax evasion, and cross-border smuggling, requiring specialized expertise beyond traditional policing.

In recent developments, the Election Commission has issued detailed instructions and Standard Operating Procedures for the proactive identification of Expenditure Sensitive Constituencies and Expenditure Sensitive Pockets. These are geographic areas identified as being particularly prone to high levels of illicit spending and corrupt electoral practices. The State Police Department plays a pivotal role in this identification process. The Chief Electoral Officer of a state or union territory is responsible for formally designating a constituency as Expenditure Sensitive based on a thorough analysis of its history, demographic profile, and past developmental patterns. This decision is not made in isolation but follows extensive discussions with District Election Officers, the State Police Department, and the various enforcement agencies participating in the monitoring ecosystem. To institutionalize this process, the Chief Electoral Officer organizes a strategic meeting with the State Level Nodal Officers of all relevant enforcement agencies at least six months before the term of the Legislative Assembly or Lok Sabha is set to expire. The objective of this early engagement is to map out sensitivities based on two primary dimensions: functional sensitivities, which relate to the main commodities used for corruption such as cash, liquor, drugs, precious metals, and freebies; and area-wise sensitivities, which analyze risks at the district level, groups of districts, or specific Assembly Constituency levels.

To aid in this identification, a checklist of indicative empirical parameters is provided, though it is understood that this list is not exhaustive and may be expanded based on specific local intelligence deliberated by the Chief Electoral Officer's office in consultation with enforcement Nodal Officers. One key parameter involves analyzing the volume of cash, liquor, drugs, narcotics, precious metals, jewelry, and freebies seized or reported during the last two general elections for the Assembly or Lok Sabha. Areas with a history of high volumes of such items are flagged as high risk. Another critical factor is the amount of seizures occurring in the six months immediately preceding the official announcement of the election, as a surge in illicit activity during this pre-code period often indicates preparedness for large-scale voter inducement. Geographic factors are also heavily weighted; constituencies that share state borders or international borders with neighboring states, union territories, or countries are identified as Expenditure Sensitive if there is a historical record of seizures or incidents during past elections, acknowledging the ease of smuggling contraband across these boundaries. Furthermore, a constituency containing a significant number of smaller Expenditure Sensitive Pockets within it is automatically elevated to the status of an Expenditure Sensitive Constituency. Financial indicators from the banking sector also serve as vital evidence; information obtained from the Reserve Bank of India regarding treasury chests that demonstrate a twenty percent or greater increase in the demand for currency notes in the six months leading up to the election announcement can trigger the designation of a constituency as sensitive. Similarly, banking institutions monitor for unusually high numbers of online or digital transactions characterized by funds moving from a few source accounts to many recipient accounts, typically involving small denominations. The emergence of such patterns six months before the probable date of polling suggests organized efforts to distribute money digitally to evade detection, thereby marking the area for heightened scrutiny.

The process of identifying and managing election-related sensitivities begins with a high-level coordination effort led by the Chief Electoral Officer Office, commonly referred to as the CEO Office. This office does not act in isolation but rather collaborates closely with State Nodal Officers who represent various enforcement agencies across the region. These enforcement agencies include bodies responsible for maintaining law and order, financial compliance, and security during the electoral process. The primary objective of this initial consultation is to compile a comprehensive list of broad sensitivities that could potentially disrupt the fairness or smooth conduct of upcoming elections. Once these overarching concerns are gathered and analyzed, the findings are broken down into specific geographical units known as districts. These district-wise findings are then formally communicated to the District Election Officers, or DEOs, who serve as the key administrative figures at the local level. Upon receiving this information, the DEOs are tasked with initiating a deeper layer of analysis by establishing a specialized body known as the District Intelligence Committee. This committee is not a permanent fixture but is formed specifically for this purpose, comprising senior officers from various enforcement agencies who operate directly within that district. Their role is to discuss the preliminary findings provided by the state level, seek additional ground-level inputs, and refine the understanding of local risks through collective expertise.

There are, however, specific logistical exceptions to this standard operating procedure that must be accounted for to ensure no gaps in intelligence gathering. In certain instances involving Central Agencies, which are national-level enforcement bodies, the physical presence of their personnel within a particular state may be extremely limited or sparse. When the strength of such an agency in a state is too low to have representative officers available in every district, the standard formation of the District Intelligence Committee faces a practical challenge. To address this, the protocol allows for flexibility wherein the District Intelligence Committee may bypass the need for district-level officers from those specific under-staffed agencies. Instead, the committee is authorized to seek necessary inputs and expert opinions directly from the State Level Nodal Officers of those respective Central Agencies. This ensures that even when local representation is absent, the intelligence gathered remains robust and informed by the highest available authority within that specific enforcement chain, thereby maintaining the integrity of the assessment process without being hindered by staffing constraints.

Once the District Intelligence Committees have completed their deliberations and gathered all requisite local intelligence, they are required to funnel their consolidated inputs back to the CEO Office. This return of information is governed by a strict and mathematically defined timeline to ensure sufficient lead time for subsequent planning stages. The stipulated deadline for this submission is calculated based on historical electoral data, specifically set at five months prior to the poll month of the corresponding previous elections. For example, if the last election in that constituency was held in May, the inputs must be submitted by December of the preceding year. This rigid scheduling is critical because it creates a structured window for the CEO Office to process the incoming data. Following the receipt of these district-level reports, the CEO Office undertakes the task of compiling detailed lists that incorporate not just the raw data but also significant observations and analytical comments. This compiled document serves as a strategic roadmap and must be forwarded to the Election Commission, the supreme governing body for elections, within a subsequent deadline. This second deadline is set at four months prior to the poll month of the corresponding previous election, effectively giving the Commission one month to review the CEO Office's synthesis before finalizing strategies.

The document generated through this rigorous process carries a classification of confidential, reflecting the sensitive nature of the information it contains regarding potential electoral vulnerabilities. This confidential list is organized meticulously by Expenditure Sensitive Constituencies, abbreviated as ESCs, and further categorized by Assembly Constituency, which are the smaller electoral divisions within a district. An Expenditure Sensitive Constituency is essentially a geographic area identified as having a high risk of excessive monetary influence, bribery, or illicit spending that could sway voter behavior. For each listed constituency, the document details the specific nature of the sensitivity, describing exactly what kind of threat or challenge is anticipated. Furthermore, the list goes beyond mere identification of problems; it explicitly outlines preparedness measures, answering the question of what actions need to be taken, and defines strategic approaches to overcome these challenges, addressing how these actions should be implemented. This dual focus on diagnosis and remediation ensures that the election machinery is not only aware of the risks but is also equipped with a pre-approved action plan to neutralize them effectively.

Adherence to these timelines is not merely an administrative formality but a fundamental requirement for the success of the electoral monitoring framework. The list and the accompanying observations received from the Chief Electoral Officers exactly four months in advance of the expiry of the Legislative Assembly of the State or Union Territory, or the Lok Sabha, are essential for making adequate logistical and operational arrangements. This lead time is indispensable for quality monitoring, allowing authorities to deploy resources, train personnel, and set up surveillance mechanisms well before the election schedule is officially announced. Without this four-month buffer, the ability to respond proactively would be severely compromised, forcing authorities into a reactive mode that could fail to prevent malpractices. The early availability of this data enables the election administration to anticipate hotspots of vulnerability and allocate superior resources to those areas, ensuring that the principle of free and fair elections is upheld through preemptive rather than corrective measures.

For constituencies identified as Expenditure Sensitive Constituencies, the deployment of human and technical resources is significantly intensified compared to normal constituencies. In such high-risk areas, the administrative structure may include up to two Assistant Expenditure Observers instead of the usual single observer. These assistants are dedicated officials tasked with monitoring financial transactions and campaign expenditures to detect any violations of spending limits. In addition to increased human oversight, the number of Flying Squads is augmented. Flying Squads are rapid response teams capable of moving quickly across the constituency to intercept illegal cash, liquor, or other inducements meant for voters. Similarly, the density of Static Surveillance Teams is increased; these are fixed posts established at strategic locations such as major roads, borders, and entry points to monitor movement continuously. Furthermore, the deployment of Video Surveillance Teams is expanded to ensure comprehensive visual coverage of sensitive pockets. All these additional resources are deployed over and above the standard complement assigned to the remaining, non-sensitive constituencies, creating a layered defense system against electoral malpractice in the most vulnerable areas.

The final output of this entire exercise, the list of Expenditure Sensitive Constituencies, must be furnished in a strictly prescribed format to ensure uniformity and clarity across all regions. This format requires specific columns of information to be filled out systematically. The first column denotes the serial number for easy reference. The second column requires the full name of the Expenditure Sensitive Constituency, clearly identifying the electoral division. The third column specifies the name of the District in which this constituency is located, providing geographical context. The fourth column mandates that the ESC designation be justified based on empirical criteria, meaning the selection must be backed by observable data, past records, or quantifiable evidence rather than subjective opinion. The fifth column requires the identification of the specific agency or agencies that mentioned or recommended the criteria used for this designation, ensuring accountability and traceability of the intelligence source. Finally, the format includes a section for a detailed note on the nature of the sensitivity of the ESC, where a comprehensive narrative description is provided to explain the specific types of threats, historical patterns of malpractice, or current intelligence indicators that make this constituency particularly sensitive. This structured approach ensures that every decision to label a constituency as sensitive is transparent, evidence-based, and actionable for the field teams who will eventually execute the monitoring strategy.

The Standard Operating Procedure for the identification of Expenditure Sensitive Pockets, often abbreviated as ESPs, establishes a rigorous framework designed to maintain the integrity of electoral processes by pinpointing geographic areas where illegal financial inducements are most likely to occur. This procedure mandates that the District Election Officer, who serves as the primary administrative authority for elections within a district, must collaborate closely with the Superintendent of Police, the head of law enforcement in the district, and the Expenditure Observer, an independent official deployed specifically to monitor campaign spending. These three key figures are required to jointly identify these sensitive pockets during the very first visit of the Expenditure Observer to the constituency, ensuring that surveillance measures are established at the earliest possible stage. The criteria for designating an area as an Expenditure Sensitive Pocket are multifaceted and rely heavily on intelligence-driven insights. Specifically, the inputs provided by the District Intelligence Committee, a body responsible for gathering and analyzing local security and criminal data, must be thoroughly considered. Areas that have historically demonstrated a high volume of complaints or recorded incidents involving the distribution of cash, alcohol, free gifts, or other material benefits during previous election cycles are prime candidates for this designation. Furthermore, officials must review empirical criteria outlined in official lists regarding the marking of Expenditure Sensitive Constituencies to ensure a comprehensive assessment. Once identified, these pockets are subjected to continuous, round-the-clock surveillance operations conducted twenty-four hours a day, seven days a week. This intense monitoring is carried out by specialized units known as Flying Squads, which are mobile teams capable of rapid deployment to various locations, and Static Surveillance Teams, which are fixed posts established to monitor specific high-risk zones. This level of vigilance is particularly critical during the final three days leading up to the polling date, a period historically associated with a surge in illicit activities. To bolster the effectiveness of these Static Surveillance Teams during this crucial window, the protocol requires the integration of personnel from Central Paramilitary Forces, which are federal armed police units deployed to ensure neutrality and enhance security capabilities.

The operational guidelines for these activities are deeply rooted in the Compendium of Instructions on Election Expenditure Monitoring, a comprehensive document published and regularly updated by the Election Commission of India. This compendium, accessible via the Commission's official digital portal, details the extensive responsibilities assigned to the Police Department in the broader ecosystem of election monitoring. It is important to note that this document undergoes annual revisions to incorporate new strategies and address emerging challenges, with specific chapters dedicated to Standard Operating Procedures for Flying Squads and Static Surveillance Teams intended primarily for police perusal. Effective preparation and execution of actions against the flow of inducements require adherence to several strategic points. Authorities must ensure that the requisitioning of police manpower accounts for adequate strength in both Flying Squads and Static Surveillance Teams to prevent gaps in coverage. A critical aspect of this strategy involves the mapping of border check posts in coordination with neighboring states to address border sensitivity, recognizing that illicit materials often cross jurisdictional lines. Consequently, border check-posts must be equipped with Closed-Circuit Television cameras to facilitate effective real-time monitoring. Timely coordination meetings must be held at the district, sub-division, and police station levels with counterparts in adjoining states to synchronize efforts. Officials are also tasked with analyzing and mapping the specific routes used for the transportation of liquor, drugs, and other inducements. To enhance the sophistication of these operations, cross-functional training sessions should be conducted involving agencies such as the Income Tax Department and the Goods and Services Tax network, fostering inter-agency cooperation. When filing First Information Reports, which are formal police documents recording the occurrence of a cognizable offense, investigators must include detailed trails of the flow and routes of these inducements. Furthermore, there must be a swift mechanism for sharing information regarding interceptions with the Income Tax Department and other relevant bodies. Specifically, when Flying Squads or Static Surveillance Teams intercept cash amounts totaling ten lakhs or more during the enforcement of the Model Code of Conduct, and where no direct criminality is immediately attached to the possession, immediate communication with the Income Tax Department is mandatory to investigate potential tax evasion or unaccounted wealth.

The scope of monitoring extends to a granular level, requiring the mapping of expenditure vulnerability across all administrative tiers, starting from individual polling stations, moving up to expenditure sensitive pockets, then to assembly constituencies, and finally to the district level. This hierarchical monitoring ensures that no area is left unchecked. Additionally, authorities must compile a list of individuals known to influence voters through various modes of inducement well in advance of the election, placing them under tight vigilance to prevent them from executing their plans. Special focus must be directed toward monitoring crucial transit and gathering points, keeping locations such as railway stations, trucks, buses, and even ambulances under strict surveillance, as these are often exploited for the covert movement of illicit goods. Historical data analysis plays a pivotal role in resource allocation; districts that witnessed significant seizures of cash in the previous two election cycles should be subject to special vigil, as they exhibit a higher probability of recurrence. The element of surprise is a tactical necessity for Static Surveillance Teams, ensuring that their functioning cannot be predicted or circumvented by those attempting to violate election laws. The progress of electoral offenses from previous elections must be monitored promptly to identify patterns and repeat offenders. A specific list of absconding individuals and those with warrants related to liquor cases must be prepared and shared with bordering states to prevent their escape or re-entry. In collaboration with the State Excise Department, which regulates the production and sale of alcohol, main and traditional routes for the inflow of liquor and drugs must be clearly marked and properly guarded. Entry points, known locally as Nakas, must also be strongly guarded to stem the tide of contraband. The strategy directs a focused effort on the arrest of kingpins, stockists, and distributors of liquor and drugs, aiming to create an effective deterrent by targeting the supply chain rather than just low-level carriers. Furthermore, pre-embarkation checks at commercial airports, helipads, and non-commercial airfields must be rigorously enforced during the election process, adhering strictly to the frisking and baggage checking procedures mandated by the Election Commission to prevent the air transport of illicit funds or substances.

To systematically curb the menace of money power influencing democratic outcomes, the Election Commission has instituted the Election Seizure Management System, a technological solution designed to streamline the reporting and management of seized assets. Recognizing that curbing financial malfeasance requires a multi-agency approach, the Commission requisitions the services of a vast array of enforcement agencies. These include the Central Board of Direct Taxes and the Central Board of Indirect Taxes and Customs, which handle tax-related investigations; the State Police Department and State Excise Department for local law enforcement and liquor control; the Enforcement Department and the Directorate of Revenue Intelligence for financial crimes; the Financial Intelligence Unit-India for tracking monetary transactions; the Narcotics Control Bureau for drug-related offenses; and the Department of Posts, Railway Protection Force, and various paramilitary forces such as the Border Security Force, Central Industrial Security Force, Indo Tibetan Border Police, Sashastra Seema Bal, Assam Rifles, and the Indian Coast Guard. Historically, the process of compiling seizure reports from these diverse agencies was manual, undertaken by the Chief Electoral Officer who would collect data and send daily compiled reports to the Commission via email or post. This manual procedure proved to be cumbersome, prone to delays, and lacked the capability for real-time analysis, as the complexity of aggregating data from numerous sources consumed significant time and resources. To address these inefficiencies, the Election Commission developed a specialized software application known as the Election Seizure Management System. This system was deployed for the first time during the general elections held in Mizoram, Chhattisgarh, Madhya Pradesh, Rajasthan, and Telangana in the year 2023. The implementation of this software ensures that seizure efforts from the field are reflected in real time, thereby eliminating redundancy and duplication of data. It facilitates seamless coordination among the various enforcement agencies involved and provides a robust mechanism for record-keeping, ultimately enhancing the transparency and efficacy of election expenditure monitoring.

To ensure the integrity and transparency of electoral processes, a systematic digital framework has been established for field-level functionaries operating within the Police Department. These officers, who serve as the primary operational units on the ground, are issued unique login credentials that grant them access to a secure data entry system. This digital access is not merely administrative but serves a critical function in recording every action taken by law enforcement from the precise moment an election is officially announced until the final conclusion of the polling process. The primary objective of this digitization initiative is to create an immutable and chronological record of all activities related to the seizure of items, such as illicit cash, liquor, or other materials intended to influence voters. By converting these physical enforcement actions into digital data points, the system facilitates real-time monitoring by higher authorities, ensuring that every seizure is logged, justified, and tracked throughout the election lifecycle. This approach minimizes the risk of documentation errors, prevents the loss of records, and provides a clear audit trail that can be reviewed to verify compliance with electoral laws.

A central pillar of these operational guidelines is the strict mandate to avoid high-handedness or excessive force by police authorities during their duties. The term high-handedness refers to the arbitrary or oppressive use of authority without regard for legal constraints or public sentiment. In the context of elections, where public trust is paramount, police officials are instructed to exercise the maximum possible restraint when conducting checks and seizing goods. This directive acknowledges the potential for power to be misused under the guise of maintaining order and explicitly commands officers to prioritize de-escalation and proportionality. The underlying principle is that the legitimacy of the election depends not only on the outcome but also on the fairness of the environment in which it takes place. Therefore, any display of aggression or unnecessary assertiveness by law enforcement is viewed as a violation of protocol that could undermine public confidence in the democratic process.

Furthermore, specific provisions are in place to prevent the harassment of the general public. Harassment in this context refers to any conduct that causes undue distress, intimidation, or inconvenience to citizens under the false pretext of mandatory poll-related checks. While the police have a duty to enforce election laws, they must not exploit this responsibility to target individuals unfairly or to conduct fishing expeditions without probable cause. The guidelines emphasize that the mere existence of an election does not grant unlimited powers to stop, question, or search citizens at will. Every interaction must be grounded in genuine suspicion or established procedural requirements rather than serving as a tool for coercion or intimidation. This protection is vital for safeguarding civil liberties and ensuring that voters feel safe and free from fear when participating in the electoral exercise.

All activities involving frisking, checking, and seizure must be executed in strict adherence to the procedures laid down under relevant statutes and the specific directions issued by the Election Commission. Frisking involves a superficial search of a person's outer clothing to detect weapons or concealed items, while checking may refer to the inspection of vehicles or premises, and seizure denotes the legal confiscation of prohibited items. These actions are not discretionary; they are bound by a rigid legal framework designed to balance security needs with individual rights. Officers must follow every step of the prescribed protocol, which typically includes establishing reasonable grounds for suspicion, announcing their intent, conducting the search in the presence of witnesses where required, and preparing detailed inventories of seized items. Deviation from these established legal pathways renders the action invalid and exposes the officer to disciplinary proceedings. The directions from the Commission serve as the supreme operational manual, overriding any local conventions or informal practices that might contradict national standards.

In the event that any grievance arises from these enforcement activities, a robust mechanism for immediate redressal has been instituted. A grievance refers to any formal complaint lodged by a citizen alleging misconduct, procedural violations, or unfair treatment during a search or seizure. Such complaints are not to be ignored or delayed but must be referred immediately to the District Grievance Committee. This committee is a specialized body constituted at the district level specifically to handle election-related disputes and complaints against authorities. Its role is to provide a swift and impartial forum where citizens can seek justice without having to navigate the complex and time-consuming regular judicial system. The requirement for immediate referral underscores the urgency of resolving conflicts during the sensitive election period, ensuring that any allegation of wrongdoing is investigated promptly and that corrective measures are taken before the situation escalates or affects the voting atmosphere.

Accountability and identification are further reinforced through strict dress code and identification protocols for enforcement agency personnel. When carrying out election-related searches and seizures, officers are mandated to carry their official identity cards and must be in full uniform whenever applicable. The identity card serves as proof of the officer's authorization and specific role, allowing citizens to verify that the individual interacting with them is a legitimate representative of the state. The requirement for uniforms ensures visibility and reduces the ambiguity of plainclothes operations, which can often lead to confusion or fear among the public. If an officer is not in uniform due to the nature of their specific assignment, the display of the ID becomes even more critical. This transparency is designed to prevent impersonation and to assure the public that the actions being taken are official, sanctioned, and subject to oversight.

Finally, to create an objective record and shield both the public and the police from baseless accusations, all search and seizure activities must be videographed. Videography involves the continuous audio-visual recording of the entire procedure, from the initial approach to the final sealing of seized items. This technological safeguard acts as an unbiased witness, capturing the exact words spoken, the manner in which the search was conducted, and the condition of the items involved. By mandating video documentation, the system effectively neutralizes potential allegations of fabrication, theft, or brutality, as the footage provides irrefutable evidence of what transpired. This practice not only protects citizens from malpractice but also safeguards honest officers from false claims, thereby fostering a climate of mutual accountability. The combination of digital logging, strict procedural adherence, grievance redressal mechanisms, mandatory identification, and compulsory videography forms a comprehensive ecosystem aimed at conducting free, fair, and fear-free elections.

The role of police forces in the arrangement and execution of elections is a cornerstone of democratic integrity, encompassing a comprehensive timeline that includes pre-poll, poll, and post-poll phases. Polling stands as the most critical event within the entire election process, serving as the moment where the will of the electorate is physically cast and recorded. Consequently, it is imperative that all activities occurring before, during, and after the voting day are conducted with strict adherence to established laws and prescribed procedural guidelines. This rigorous framework ensures that the election remains free, fair, and untouched by coercion or malpractice. The responsibilities of law enforcement agencies are segmented into distinct temporal phases, each requiring specific operational protocols to maintain order and security throughout the constituency.

During the pre-poll phase, which occurs in the days leading up to the actual voting day, several strategic actions must be undertaken to prepare the environment for a peaceful election. The first priority is to ensure that all security forces have reached their designated polling stations in full accordance with the approved force deployment plan. This logistical precision is vital to guarantee that no station is left vulnerable. Once deployed, these forces must carry out effective and focused flag marches. These marches serve multiple purposes, including area domination, which establishes a visible security presence to deter potential troublemakers, point patrolling to monitor specific high-risk locations, and other confidence-building measures designed to reassure the general public that the area is secure. Furthermore, specialized teams such as Static Surveillance Teams, Flying Squads, and Quick Reaction Teams are formed to conduct continuous surveillance. These units are tasked with carrying out effective enforcement activities and taking preventive action against any elements that might attempt to disrupt the electoral process. A crucial aspect of this phase is the enforcement of arms control. A ban on carrying licensed arms remains in full effect from the announcement of the election until the final declaration of results. Additionally, no new licenses for arms are issued during this period to prevent the influx of weapons into the region. Police forces must maintain a strict vigil, conducting thorough checks of lorries, light vehicles, and all other forms of transport. Preferably, this is achieved by establishing equipped check posts at strategic entry points. This screening process begins from the day the elections are announced and continues uninterrupted to ensure that no undesirable elements, nor any arms and ammunition, are transported into the constituency from outside areas. If such items or individuals are detected, the police are empowered to apprehend them and take appropriate legal action immediately.

As the process transitions into the polling day itself, the focus shifts to stringent law and order measures within and around the polling stations. The District Election Officer and the Superintendent of Police hold the responsibility of briefing the Central Armed Police Forces and state police personnel about the specific provisions of Section 131 of the Representation of the People Act, 1951. This section of the law outlines the powers and duties of police officers in maintaining order at polling stations. To ensure these legal provisions are effectively implemented, there must be proper coordination between the Presiding Officer, who manages the internal proceedings of the booth, and the security forces posted there. Spatial restrictions are strictly enforced to create a neutral zone around voting facilities. No election booth belonging to any candidate is permitted to be set up within a radius of two hundred meters from a polling station. This rule applies even in complex scenarios where more than one polling station is located within the same building or premises; in such cases, only one election booth per candidate is allowed for that entire group of stations, and it must be situated beyond the two-hundred-meter distance from the premises. Furthermore, no electioneering activities, such as campaigning, shouting slogans, or distributing literature, are allowed within this two-hundred-meter exclusion zone on the date of the poll.

Security protocols regarding electronic communication are equally rigorous. Within a one-hundred-meter perimeter surrounding the polling stations, an area legally defined as the Polling Station Neighborhood under Section 130 of the Representation of the People Act, 1951, as well as inside the polling station itself, the possession and use of mobile phones, wireless sets, and similar communication devices are prohibited. This restriction applies to everyone except for observers appointed by the Election Commission and authorized election or police officials who require these devices for official duties. The objective is to prevent real-time coordination of booth capturing or other fraudulent activities. Regarding the possession of arms, Section 134B of the Representation of the People Act, 1951, explicitly states that no person, except those expressly permitted by the section, may carry any arms or indulge in a show of force within the polling stations or their immediate neighborhood. This prohibition is absolute; no individual, even if they are a recipient of security protection from any government agency or private source, is allowed to enter a polling station or its neighborhood accompanied by their security personnel. This ensures that the voting environment remains devoid of any intimidation or display of military power.

The deployment of security forces follows a precise hierarchy and operational plan. The Commissioner of Police or the Superintendent of Police is responsible for deploying police forces, including the Central Armed Police Forces, according to the approved District Deployment Plan for both static guard duties and mobile patrols. A critical rule mandates that all forces assigned to polling station security must take their positions and assume control of their respective stations on the day prior to the poll day. This early arrival allows for familiarization with the terrain and immediate readiness. In the event that the Central Armed Police Forces have not reached an assigned polling station for any reason, the polling process in that specific station cannot commence. This underscores the non-negotiable nature of central force presence in ensuring fairness. At stations where these central forces are deployed, one personnel must be stationed at the door of the polling station, either in a static standing position or moving in an oscillating pattern, specifically to observe the proceedings taking place inside. This requirement was reinforced by a landmark judgment of the Supreme Court of India in the case of Janak Singh versus Ram Das Rai and Others. While the central forces guard the booths, the Company Commanders of these forces are also expected to move through their assigned sectors to maintain area domination and continue confidence-building measures among the populace. Distinctly, the State Police Force retains sole responsibility for maintaining general law and order inside and outside the broader polling station premises, which differs from the specific booth security handled by central forces. The state police are strictly prohibited from replacing the Central Armed Police Forces at the polling stations in contravention of the District Deployment Plan under any circumstances. Furthermore, no officer of the local state police, whether alone or with a contingent, is permitted to position themselves at the polling station to exercise supervision or control over the central forces deployed there, preserving the chain of command and operational independence of the central units.

General restrictions under the Model Code of Conduct also impose specific limitations on vehicular movement and voter transport on poll day to prevent corrupt practices. The regulations allow only one vehicle each for a candidate, their election agent, and for the candidate's workers or party workers. Strict capacity limits are enforced, permitting no more than five persons, including the driver, in any such authorized vehicle. It is classified as a corrupt practice for these vehicles to carry voters to the polling stations, and doing so constitutes an electoral offense punishable under Section 133 of the Representation of the People Act, 1951. To ensure transparency and easy identification, permits issued by the District Election Officer must be prominently displayed on the windscreen of all authorized vehicles. The provision of free conveyance to voters for traveling to and from the polling stations by a candidate or their agent is strictly forbidden and categorized as a corrupt practice. Such actions undermine the principle of a free vote by potentially inducement or coercion through transportation logistics. Any violation of these transport rules may result in the offender being booked under the relevant legal provisions, ensuring that the financial or logistical advantage does not influence the electoral outcome. These detailed measures collectively form a robust shield around the electoral process, safeguarding the sanctity of the vote from the initial deployment of forces to the final casting of ballots.

The comprehensive restrictions outlined herein are designed to apply universally to every conceivable type of vehicle that operates via mechanical propulsion or any other means of power, ensuring no loophole exists for evasion. This broad mandate explicitly encompasses a wide array of transportation modes including but not limited to taxies, privately owned automobiles, commercial trucks, agricultural tractors whether towing trailers or operating independently, auto-rickshaws, electric rickshaws, motor scooters, motorcycles, mini buses, and station wagons among others. The temporal scope of these regulations is strictly defined to commence twenty-four hours prior to the scheduled time for the closure of polling activities and shall remain in full force until the entire polling process is conclusively finished. Any individual found violating these directives shall face severe penal consequences drawn from multiple legal frameworks simultaneously. Specifically, offenders will be prosecuted under the provisions of the Representation of the People Act of 1951 as well as the Indian Penal Code, with additional punitive measures imposed under the Motor Vehicles Act. A critical enforcement mechanism involves the immediate confiscation of any vehicle utilized in contravention of these orders, serving as a strong deterrent against misuse during the sensitive election period.

Despite the stringent bans, specific exemptions are carved out to ensure essential mobility and democratic participation are not unduly hindered. Private vehicles retain their utility for owners engaging in personal matters completely unrelated to electioneering activities. Furthermore, owners are permitted to utilize their private vehicles to transport themselves or immediate family members to polling booths for the sole purpose of exercising their franchise, provided such travel does not involve entering the restricted zone extending two hundred meters around any polling station. Essential service vehicles constitute another vital category of exemption, allowing hospital vans, ambulances, milk delivery trucks, water tankers, electricity emergency response units, police vehicles on active duty, and officers officially deployed for election responsibilities to continue operating freely. Public transport carriages such as buses are authorized to ply only between fixed terminals and along predetermined routes to maintain basic public transit without facilitating unauthorized movement. Taxis, three-wheeled scooters, and rickshaws may operate exclusively for indispensable journeys to airports, railway stations, interstate bus stands, or hospitals where travel cannot be avoided. Special consideration is given to sick or disabled individuals who are allowed to use private vehicles for their personal medical or mobility needs. Additionally, government officers traveling to reach their assigned duty points are permitted to use vehicles to fulfill their official obligations.

Strict communication and campaign protocols are enforced within the immediate vicinity of polling stations to preserve the sanctity and secrecy of the vote. The use of cellular phones, cordless phones, and similar communication devices is prohibited within one hundred meters of a polling station, with the sole exception being officers who are on official duty. Voter identification slips must adhere to a standardized format, appearing as plain white slips devoid of any candidate names, political party markings, or electoral symbols to prevent any form of inducement or bias. The display of campaign-related posters, banners, or propaganda materials is strictly forbidden within a radius of one hundred meters from any polling booth. During the actual polling hours, the Presiding Officer and all supporting staff are required to keep their mobile phones on silent mode while inside the polling station. If communication becomes absolutely necessary, they must step outside the polling booth premises to make calls. The Booth Level Officer is mandated to be stationed externally at the Voter Assistance Booth located near the polling station, keeping their mobile phone switched on and maintaining access to the alphabetical roll locator to assist voters efficiently.

Maintaining order and silence around polling stations is paramount, leading to a total prohibition on loudspeakers, megaphones, and any apparatus capable of amplifying or reproducing the human voice within one hundred meters of a polling station. Disorderly conduct, including shouting or acting in a disruptive manner, is strictly banned in this zone to ensure a peaceful environment for voters. Access control is rigorously enforced, permitting entry within the one hundred-meter perimeter only to authorized security personnel. Even security personnel attached to specific individuals are generally barred from entering the interior of the polling booth itself. A narrow exception exists for individuals covered under Z-plus security categorization, wherein exactly one security person dressed in simple civilian clothes and carrying one concealed firearm is permitted to accompany the protectee. Following the conclusion of polling, the process moves to the counting of votes, which represents one of the most critical stages of the entire election cycle. Consequently, extreme vigilance is exercised not only regarding the physical counting of ballots but also in the maintenance of law and order throughout the constituency.

To safeguard the integrity of the counting process, orders are typically issued under Section 144 of the Criminal Procedure Code of 1973. This legal provision empowers authorities to ban unlawful assemblies and prohibit the holding of public meetings during the day designated for vote counting. Such orders are issued by the District Magistrate or another competent authority and become effective from two days prior to the counting day until one day after, covering all areas and constituencies involved in the polls. Under these Section 144 restrictions, gatherings of more than five persons moving or assembling together in the specified area are not permitted. In alignment with statutory provisions contained in Section 135C of the Representation of the People Act of 1951, a Dry Day is declared and notified under relevant state laws for the duration of the counting day, prohibiting the sale and consumption of alcohol to prevent intoxication-related disturbances.

The security architecture for protecting strong rooms, where electronic voting machines and ballots are stored, employs a robust two-tier guarding system. The first tier constitutes the innermost perimeter and is guarded exclusively by the Central Armed Police Forces. This involves a twenty-four-seven armed guard presence. In areas not affected by Left Wing Extremism, a minimum of one section of Central Armed Police Forces personnel remains on duty around the clock within the strong room facilities. To achieve this continuous coverage, a minimum of one platoon of Central Armed Police Forces is allocated to guard the strong room. In regions characterized by Left Wing Extremism, militancy, or insurgency, the security deployment is intensified, with one platoon manned in each of three shifts to ensure uninterrupted protection. The second tier of security is provided by the State Armed Police. Strict protocols govern entry into the inner perimeter, and no individual is allowed to cross this boundary without adhering to the established security procedures.

Security arrangements in and around counting centers are further reinforced through a three-tier cordoning system established across all counting premises. This layered approach is designed specifically to prevent the entry of unauthorized persons into the sensitive areas where votes are being tallied. The outer layers filter general access, while the inner layers provide increasingly restricted zones accessible only to authorized counting agents and officials. Finally, to manage public sentiment and prevent potential unrest following the announcement of outcomes, victory processions that occur post-declaration of results may be subject to restriction or regulation by local authorities. This measure ensures that celebrations do not devolve into chaotic assemblies or violate the ongoing prohibitions on large gatherings, thereby maintaining public order during the transitional period between counting and the final certification of election results.

To maintain public peace and prevent violent confrontations between rival political factions, robust and meticulously planned arrangements must be established well in advance of any electoral event. Given the multifaceted nature of election management, which requires simultaneous action across various functional dimensions, it is absolutely imperative that all officials responsible for law and order at the state, district, and constituency levels possess a comprehensive understanding of the critical activities and procedural steps necessary during the pre-poll, poll, and post-poll phases. These functionaries must be thoroughly prepared to execute their duties in strict adherence to the guidelines issued by the Election Commission of India, ensuring that the entire process remains orderly and secure. The conduct of free, fair, and peaceful elections stands as a fundamental characteristic and non-negotiable requirement of a functioning democracy. This monumental task involves strenuous activities carried out by a vast election machinery comprising officials and staff drawn from various government departments, with security forces playing a pivotal role. To guarantee the integrity of this process, the Election Commission invariably drafts and deputes members of the Central Armed Police Forces, commonly known as CAPF, to election duties. This authority is derived directly from Article 324 of the Constitution of India, which empowers the Commission to superintend, direct, and control the preparation of electoral rolls and the conduct of all elections. Furthermore, this practice is reinforced by landmark orders from the Honorable Supreme Court of India, specifically in the case of Election Commission of India versus Union of India and Others, Writ Petition number 606 of 1993. The decision to deploy these forces is based on a rigorous assessment of the law and order situation on the ground, gathered from multiple intelligence sources, alongside other relevant inputs regarding potential risks or vulnerabilities in specific regions.

The legal framework governing the status of these security personnel during elections is clearly defined under Section 28 A of the Representation of the People Act, 1951. This statute mandates that all officers and personnel from both the State Police and the Central Armed Police Forces, once deployed for election-related duties, are formally deputed to the Election Commission of India. This deputation period commences from the date the official notification calling for the election is issued and continues until the date the entire election process is declared complete. During this specific timeframe, these officers and personnel operate entirely under the superintendence, direction, and control of the Election Commission of India, effectively placing them outside the regular chain of command of their respective state or central ministries for the duration of their election assignment. It is essential to understand the scope of the term Central Armed Police Forces, or CAPFs, within this context. The definition is broad and inclusive, encompassing all Central Armed Police Forces, State Armed Police Forces, battalions of the Indian Reserve Battalion, and the Railway Protection Force. Additionally, it includes any other force that the Ministry of Home Affairs may deploy upon the specific direction of the Election Commission of India solely for the purpose of facilitating elections. This expansive definition ensures that the Commission has access to a wide array of trained manpower capable of handling diverse security challenges across the nation.

To ensure seamless coordination between the central government and local authorities, a structured hierarchy of coordinators is established. The Ministry of Home Affairs is responsible for appointing a State Level Force Coordinator, abbreviated as SLFC, for every state or union territory that is scheduled to hold elections. This appointment must be made six months in advance of the polling dates to allow sufficient time for planning and logistical preparation. The officer appointed to this crucial role must hold a rank not below that of an Inspector General. However, recognizing the varying scales of administrative needs, the regulations allow for flexibility in smaller states or union territories, where an officer holding the rank of Deputy Inspector General may be appointed instead. Parallel to this, the poll-bound state or union territory must appoint a State CAPF Coordinator. This individual serves to assist the State Police Nodal Officer and must also hold a rank not below that of an Inspector General of Police, or a Deputy Inspector General in the case of smaller jurisdictions. The primary function of the State CAPF Coordinator is to facilitate close and effective coordination with the State Level Force Coordinator appointed by the Ministry of Home Affairs, ensuring that the deployment strategies of the central forces align perfectly with the local security requirements and intelligence assessments.

The deployment of Central Armed Police Forces is a standard and invariant directive issued by the Commission for the conduct of General Elections and Bye-Elections to the Lok Sabha, which is the lower house of India's parliament, as well as for elections to State Legislative Assemblies. Once deployed, these forces are dedicated exclusively to election duties and shall not be kept in reserve or utilized for any other purposes unless explicitly specified by the Commission. Any deviation from this rule or any attempt to use these forces for alternative tasks requires the prior approval of the Election Commission, which must be sought through the Chief Electoral Officer of the respective state. The planning for this deployment is a collaborative effort; the District Deployment Plan must be prepared jointly by the District Election Officer and the Superintendent of Police for each district. This plan is then subjected to a rigorous vetting process by the senior-most General Observer and Police Observer assigned to the district, ensuring that the strategy is sound and comprehensive. The actual execution of the deployment, in accordance with the approved District Deployment Plan, falls under the direct responsibility of the concerned Commissioner of Police or Superintendent of Police, who must ensure that forces are positioned exactly as planned.

Strategic placement of these forces is critical to the success of the election. Central Armed Police Forces must invariably be deployed at all critical polling stations, which are locations identified as having a high risk of violence, booth capturing, or other malpractices. The deployment plan must be designed such that every single polling station is covered by an effective static duty force, meaning personnel who remain stationed at the location to deter misconduct. This static presence is further reinforced by necessary mobile patrolling units operating at the sector level, allowing for rapid response to incidents in surrounding areas. In scenarios where there is a shortage of Central Armed Police Forces, prioritization becomes essential. In such cases, the most critical polling stations must be provided with CAPF personnel, while less critical polling stations may be assigned mixed forces comprising state police or other non-CAPF units. To facilitate smooth operations, detailed information regarding the District Deployment Plan, including the district profile, route plans for movement, accommodation arrangements, transport logistics, and contact details of Nodal Officers, must be fully prepared before the arrival of the Central Armed Police Forces in the state. These details must be shared with the forces immediately upon their arrival to minimize confusion and maximize efficiency. Unlike some other security protocols, the deployment of CAPFs for elections is not required to be randomized; instead, it is based on calculated risk assessment and strategic necessity.

Special provisions are also made for the security of Very Important Persons, or VIPs, during the election campaign. In instances involving election rallies, public meetings, or roadshows featuring high-profile candidates or leaders, Central Armed Police Forces can be deployed based on the specific threat perception associated with that VIP. However, such deployment is contingent upon obtaining prior permission from the Election Commission of India to ensure that the diversion of forces does not compromise the security of polling stations or other critical areas. Furthermore, when Central Armed Police Forces are moving into areas affected by Left Wing Extremism, often referred to as LWE, or other insurgency-prone regions, they should be encouraged to carry their specialized Battle Damage Drainage Systems, known as BDDS units, along with their standard equipment and specially trained manpower. If available, these forces should also be equipped with force multipliers such as drones for aerial surveillance, Night Vision Devices abbreviated as NVD for operations in low-light conditions, and other advanced technological tools to enhance their operational effectiveness in difficult terrains. Finally, comprehensive briefing sessions are mandatory for all deployed troops. During these briefings, personnel must be informed about previous incidents of violence, mistakes made by forces in past elections, current threat perceptions, specific vulnerabilities of the area, their precise duties, and a clear list of Dos and Don'ts. The briefing must also cover the terrain and topography of the assigned area and provide important contact numbers for emergency communication. If necessary, this briefing material should be widely circulated in electronic mode to ensure that every member of the troop is fully informed and prepared to uphold the sanctity of the democratic process.

The strategic deployment of Central Armed Police Forces, commonly referred to as CAPF, is governed by a rigorous framework that prioritizes the criticality of specific geographic areas to ensure maximum effectiveness in maintaining law and order. This framework mandates a differentiated approach based on the threat landscape of the region in question. In areas identified as being affected by Left Wing Extremism, militancy, or active insurgency, the security protocol requires a robust presence, stipulating that no less than one full Section of CAPF personnel must be deployed. A Section in this context represents a standardized tactical unit capable of independent operation and significant force projection. Conversely, in regions that do not fall under the classification of extremist or insurgent influence, the deployment standard is adjusted to half a Section, reflecting a calibrated response to lower threat levels while still maintaining a visible security apparatus. This splitting of sections based on area criticality ensures that resources are allocated efficiently, with heavier concentrations in volatile zones where the risk to democratic processes and public safety is elevated.

When these forces are mobilized specifically for election duty, their role expands beyond simple security to encompass a wide array of activities designed to safeguard the integrity of the electoral process from the preparatory stages through to the final counting of votes. The pre-poll phase is particularly crucial for establishing an environment conducive to free and fair voting. During this period, which extends up to two days before the actual polling day, often denoted as P-2 in operational planning, the CAPF is tasked with conducting effective and focused flag marches. These maneuvers are not merely ceremonial but serve as vital tools for area domination, demonstrating state authority and deterrence against potential disruptors. The forces engage in point patrolling and various confidence-building measures intended to reassure the local populace. To maximize their impact, these flag marches should be conducted primarily on foot rather than using vehicles, facilitating direct interaction between the security personnel and the local community. Such engagement helps instill public confidence in the general law and order situation, signaling that the state is present and attentive. Furthermore, these units may be integrated into specialized teams such as Static Surveillance Teams, Flying Squads, or Quick Response Teams to enhance surveillance capabilities across the district.

To guide these operations effectively, the CAPF units are provided with comprehensive intelligence dossiers containing lists of known anti-social elements and maps identifying vulnerable pockets within the constituency. This information allows the forces to maintain a heightened watch on unlawful activities and preempt potential disturbances. A significant portion of the pre-poll effort is dedicated to the sanitization of routes, a technical term referring to the thorough sweeping and clearing of paths used by election officials and voters. This is especially critical in areas prone to Improvised Explosive Devices, which are often found in Left Wing Extremism and insurgency-affected zones. Sanitization involves checking for hidden threats and ensuring safe passage for all election-related movement. Additionally, every member of the force is equipped with a detailed list of precautions, including explicit dos and don'ts, to ensure standardized conduct and adherence to legal and ethical guidelines during their deployment.

On the actual day of polling, the responsibilities of the CAPF shift towards direct protection and oversight of the electoral machinery. The forces are assigned the critical duty of accompanying polling parties, along with sensitive polling materials, from their assembly points to the designated Polling Stations. Once arrived, their primary mandate includes guarding the Polling Stations themselves, securing the electronic voting machines and other materials, protecting the polling personnel who manage the vote, and keeping a continuous watch on the entire poll process to prevent any malpractice. The deployment strategy for this phase is flexible and can take several forms depending on the specific needs of the location. One method involves the static guarding of critical polling stations solely and exclusively by CAPF personnel, reserved for locations deemed high-risk. Alternatively, static guarding may be performed as part of a mixed or composite team that includes local state police forces, fostering cooperation between central and state agencies. Other deployment modes include patrolling duties on assigned routes, known as election sectors, which cover a fixed cluster of Polling Stations to provide mobile security coverage. Forces may also operate as part of Flying Squads within a defined area to respond rapidly to incidents, or they may be tasked with escorting polled Electronic Voting Machines, accompanied by polling personnel, back to Receipt Centers or Strong Rooms after the polls conclude. Beyond these specific roles, the CAPF may be assigned any other duty necessitated to ensure the purity and fidelity of the election process, acting as a versatile shield against electoral fraud and violence.

The logistical coordination of these movements is a shared responsibility, where it falls upon the District Magistrate and the Superintendent of Police concerned to ensure that both the polling parties and the static armed forces reach their designated Polling Stations according to the strict schedule. Timing is essential to prevent delays that could disenfranchise voters or create security vacuums. To further strengthen security, CAPF units designated for static duty are required to be deployed at their assigned Polling Stations at least one day prior to the actual poll day. This early arrival allows them to familiarize themselves with the terrain, establish perimeters, and settle into their positions without the chaos of election morning. Sector Officers and Magistrates are mandated to possess detailed records of the static force parties allocated to each Polling Station under their charge, as outlined in the comprehensive District Deployment Plan, ensuring accountability and clear chains of command.

Following the conclusion of voting, the post-poll activities focus heavily on the secure transportation and storage of the voting data. The polled Electronic Voting Machines and the Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail units, along with the polling personnel, must be escorted by CAPF to the Receiving Centers or Strong Rooms. The specifics of these escort missions, including routes and timing, are worked out in advance by the District Election Officer and the Commandant or Superintendent of Police in consultation with Election Observers to eliminate ambiguity. Once the machines arrive at the Strong Rooms, the CAPF is assigned the duty of guarding these facilities twenty-four hours a day, seven days a week. This continuous vigilance lasts from the moment the machines are stored until the day of counting, ensuring that the evidence of the people's verdict remains tamper-proof. Reference is made to the latest edition of the EVM Manual to ensure all technical security protocols are followed meticulously. Furthermore, adequate and fool-proof security arrangements must be established both inside and around the Counting Centers to prevent any incidents that could vitiate or invalidate the counting process. Sufficient CAPF personnel are required to be present at all Counting Centers to maintain this high level of security throughout the tabulation phase.

Further directions regarding the poll day duties of the CAPF emphasize the importance of static duty protocols. As previously noted, CAPF units must take their positions in the assigned polling stations at least one day prior to the poll to begin their static guarding responsibilities. In this capacity, they are primarily responsible for protecting the physical infrastructure of the Polling Stations, the safety of the polling parties, the security of the polling materials, and the regulation of entry into the voting premises. A specific operational requirement dictates that at least one personnel of the CAPF must position himself at the door of the Polling Station, either in a static standing mode or an oscillating moving mode, to observe the proceedings occurring inside. This requirement is grounded in legal precedent, specifically cited in the case of Janak Singh versus Ram Das Rai and Others, which underscores the necessity of external oversight to ensure procedural compliance. The CAPF staff stationed at the entrance are expected to keep a vigilant eye on activities within the station and are obligated to report any unusual observations immediately to the Officer in-Charge of the CAPF unit, the Presiding Officer, Micro Observers, Sector Officers, or General and Police Observers.

The scope of observation for CAPF personnel deployed at the entrance is clearly defined to cover specific prohibitions and requirements essential for a free and fair election. They must ensure that no unauthorized person is present inside the Polling Station at any time during the polling hours, maintaining the sanctity of the inner sanctum. They are tasked with verifying that neither the polling party nor the polling agents attempt to cast any vote when no legitimate voter is present inside the booth, thereby preventing ballot stuffing. It is strictly prohibited for any polling officer to accompany a voter into the voting compartment, a rule enforced to guarantee the secrecy of the ballot. Furthermore, CAPF personnel must ensure that no polling agent or polling officer threatens any voter or makes any gesture that could be interpreted as threatening, thus protecting voters from intimidation. The carrying of arms inside the Polling Station is absolutely forbidden to prevent coercion and violence. Additionally, the forces must be alert to prevent any silent rigging of the poll process, which refers to subtle, non-violent methods of manipulating the outcome. In scenarios where the CAPF is on static duty and faces a situation requiring additional manpower or specialized intervention, reinforcements from the local police shall be provided to support the central forces, ensuring a unified and overwhelming response to any threat against the democratic process.

When situations of disorder or disturbance arise within the boundaries of a polling station, the Presiding Officer, who is the senior election official in charge of that specific location, is legally empowered to take immediate action under Section 131 of the Representation of the People Act 1951. This legislation grants the authority to utilize the presence of Central Armed Police Forces, commonly known as CAPF, or the State Police force to manage any untoward incident that threatens the integrity, freedom, and fairness of the voting process. To ensure this power is exercised effectively, there must be seamless and proper coordination between the Presiding Officer and the security personnel from the CAPF or State Police who are posted at the polling station. It is essential that both the election officials and the security forces receive suitable briefings prior to the commencement of voting to establish a clear framework for mutual cooperation throughout the duration of the poll. The District Election Officer, often abbreviated as DEO, along with the Commissioner of Police or Superintendent of Police, holds the responsibility of briefing the CAPF and State Police units about these specific legal provisions and operational expectations. A critical rule governs the start of voting procedures: if the assigned CAPF unit has not arrived at the designated polling station for any reason whatsoever, the polling process in that specific station cannot begin under any circumstances. Furthermore, the State Police Force is strictly prohibited from replacing the CAPF units at polling stations if such an action would contradict the established District Deployment Plan, which is the strategic document outlining security arrangements. No officer from the local state police, whether accompanied by a contingent of officers or acting alone, is permitted to position themselves inside a polling station to exercise supervision or control over the CAPF personnel stationed there, ensuring that the chain of command remains distinct and unhindered by local jurisdictional interference.

Regarding the execution of mobile patrolling duties, when CAPF units are assigned responsibilities that involve moving across one or more sectors containing clusters of polling stations, often referred to as Fixed Static Teams or FST roles, the patrolling unit must ensure comprehensive supervision and guarding of all polling stations within those designated sectors. This is achieved by actively assisting or joining forces with the Sector Officers and Sector Police Officers responsible for those areas. These mobile units are required to maintain continuous communication not only with the sector-level officers but also with Micro Observers, who are independent monitors, and the Presiding Officers at individual polling stations. As part of their mandate, these mobile patrols must conduct surprise inspections of polling stations; however, they are instructed to perform these checks from the exterior without entering the inner sanctum of the voting area to avoid disrupting the secret ballot process. Any findings from these surprise checks must be promptly reported to the District Control Room and relevant senior officers. To facilitate effective operations, the mobile patrolling units of the CAPF shall be provided with a detailed list identifying critical polling stations and vulnerable localities that fall within their assigned sectors. Additionally, these units must be equipped with detailed route maps and a directory of contact details for senior officers, officials on static duty, and other officers specifically assigned to manage those sectors, ensuring rapid response capabilities and informed decision-making during patrols.

The arrangements for accommodation, transport, and other logistical support for the Central Armed Police Forces during their deployment in various states or union territories for election duty must adhere to minimum standards specified in official Instruction Number 464/INST/EPS/2023 regarding CAPFs, dated June 14, 2023. General directions concerning welfare measures mandate the advance identification of suitable lodging and the creation of a pool of mechanically sound vehicles that are customized to withstand prevailing weather conditions. These arrangements must be communicated to the respective offices of the incoming forces well in advance. The earmarked accommodation facilities must be clean, well-ventilated, and airy, equipped with essential amenities such as adequate toilets and bathrooms, sufficient lighting, electrical charging points for devices, and cooking fuel. Rigorous safety protocols require that fitness certificates and security verifications be conducted for all drivers, ensuring they are fully capable of navigating difficult terrains, a requirement that is particularly crucial in the challenging geographical landscapes of the north eastern states. Necessary arrangements for petroleum, oil, and lubricants, collectively known as POL, must be made to fuel vehicles brought by the forces themselves, as well as those provided by state authorities, continuing until the final de-induction or withdrawal of the troops from the area. Furthermore, meal packets or refreshment charges equivalent to those applicable to State Police personnel on election duty should be extended to the personnel of all forces deployed for the polls, including on the day of vote counting. If required for maintaining law and order, defense materials must be provided to the deployed forces. To ensure uninterrupted communication, SIM cards or recharge allowances may be provided to all Company Commanders and supervisory officers. Strategic planning must also include the identification of specific spots for stationing air ambulances and emergency evacuation helicopters to facilitate rapid medical response. As a significant welfare measure, the Election Commission has issued instructions mandating the provision of an honorarium to CAPF personnel deployed for election duty, and these instructions must be followed scrupulously.
