A special reference is made to challenges regarding internal security, specifically focusing on Left Wing Extremism, known as LWE, as well as Militancy and Insurgency, which represent armed rebellions or violent movements against the state that can severely disrupt electoral activities. Officers must also study the Infrastructure profile of the region, understanding the road networks, communication towers, and building structures that will impact their mobility and operations. A Preliminary post deployment Plan is developed to outline the immediate steps officers must take upon arriving at their assigned locations. This induction heavily emphasizes Vulnerability mapping and details of critical polling stations to ensure forces know exactly where the highest risks lie. In areas affected by LWE, Insurgency, or Militancy, Special Induction Training is mandated for companies, or Coys, which are military units consisting of approximately one hundred soldiers. This familiarization and induction training must be meticulously planned for all arriving forces. Pre-Induction training is conducted for a specified period to acquaint personnel with the specific routes leading to polling booths, ensuring they can navigate the terrain efficiently and safely. The curriculum includes a deep dive into tactics suited for counter-insurgency, an analysis of the local terrain, a review of incident history to learn from past conflicts, and an understanding of the modus operandi, or standard methods of operation, employed by Maoist groups and other insurgents. It is vital to instill necessary do's and don'ts, which are strict behavioral guidelines dictating what actions are permitted and prohibited while operating in these volatile zones. A significant focus is placed on counter IED training, teaching soldiers how to detect, avoid, and neutralize Improvised Explosive Devices, which are homemade bombs frequently used by insurgents. Personnel must learn the precautions to be taken and the Standard Operating Procedures, or SOPs, which are officially established step-by-step instructions to carry out operations uniformly. Understanding the specific area of deployment is crucial, and to facilitate this, Dos and Don'ts along with area-specific information brochures must be prepared well in advance and distributed to every force unit immediately upon their arrival.

The execution of this specialized training relies on District Level master trainers and State level master trainers drawn from the police department, who possess local knowledge and tactical experience. Specialized training may be customized for each district based on its unique local conditions, ensuring relevance and effectiveness. Resource persons and the best available trainers must be shortlisted much in advance and deployed specifically for this purpose to maintain high standards. To rationalize the use of scarce training resources such as venues, equipment, and expert time, training should ideally be imparted at coy clusters, where multiple units are trained together in a centralized location. Trainers are to be deputed from established training institutes to ensure professional delivery. The timeline and duration of such training are not fixed but vary depending upon the violence profile of the area, with more dangerous regions requiring longer and more intensive preparation. The training syllabus for induction is designed to span between three to seven days, with the exact length determined by the exposure of the forces to similar field threats; those with less experience may require the full week. This critical training must start immediately on the arrival of the forces to maximize preparation time and must conclude no later than twenty days prior to the date of the poll, allowing a buffer for final deployments and rest. Parallel to this is the Training of Master Trainers, also known as Training of Trainers or ToT, which covers all aforementioned topics to create a multiplier effect. These sessions are conducted by State Level master trainers or faculty from the Indian Institute of Democracy and Election Management, known as IIDEM. The target audience for ToT is a dedicated resource pool of master trainers who must be created and regularly updated. A minimum strength of at least five master trainers per district must be established to ensure adequate coverage. These trainers must be selected from the Police department and must possess direct exposure to the investigation and prosecution of electoral malpractices in their working history, ensuring they can teach from practical experience. They must receive refresher training at least four to six months prior to the scheduled elections to keep their knowledge current. The Training cell located at the state police headquarters is responsible for maintaining the list of such Master trainers and ensuring that the upgradation of their skills happens regularly through continuous education. This advanced training cycle must commence six months before the date of the poll and must be completed by four months prior to the election date, creating a ready cadre of instructors well before the operational phase begins.

General guidelines governing the conduct of all training programs emphasize practicality, accountability, and inclusivity. Hands on Training is mandatory, meaning police officers must be given direct exposure to real working EVMs and Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail systems, known as VVPAT, which provide a physical paper record of electronic votes for verification purposes. A model kit involving actual polling materials for use by polling parties must be provided at the training venue so that a detailed demonstration can be performed, allowing trainees to physically handle the equipment they will use in the field. Regarding Training Size, all training programmes should be conducted in groups of less than fifty trainees to ensure that each participant receives adequate attention and opportunity for interaction. To maintain transparency and allow for future review, all training programmes should be video recorded in their entirety. A dedicated Training counter must be established at the dispatch centre, which serves as the logistical hub for deploying personnel. This counter also functions as a Doubt clearance facility, ensuring there is a mechanism at the end of every training session for participants to ask questions and resolve uncertainties. At the level of the Superintendent of Police office, often abbreviated as SP office, a special cell should be created specifically for clearing the doubts of police staff and officers, providing a hierarchical support system. Furthermore, a Training counter and doubt clearing opportunity should be provided directly at the dispatch centre, where a master trainer with thorough knowledge about all aspects of election matters can be posted to offer immediate expert advice. Sensitization is another critical pillar, requiring that the sensitization of police personnel about the special needs of people with disability must be emphasized, ensuring that election staff are trained to assist voters with physical or cognitive impairments respectfully and effectively. A Facilitation Centre must be established to ensure proper facilitation for reporting and attendance of police personnel at training venues, removing logistical barriers to participation. Accountability for imparting training must be clearly assigned to specific officers to ensure deadlines and quality standards are met. Infrastructure at Training Centres is strictly regulated; at all training locations, proper facilities for drinking water, vehicle parking, help desk services, provisions for medical facilities, and clean toilets should be ensured to maintain the health and morale of the trainees. Finally, an Evaluation must be conducted after training to assess the effectiveness of the instruction and the competency of the participants, ensuring that only qualified personnel are deployed.

The timeline for these extensive training activities is precisely codified in the Handbook of the returning officer, which serves as the authoritative guide for election scheduling relative to the polling date, denoted as P. The Training of Police Personnel at the District Level is scheduled to start forty days before the poll, marked as P minus 40, and must end thirty-nine days before the poll, or P minus 39, creating a focused one-day window for district-wide instruction. At the Sub Divisional Level, which is a smaller administrative unit within a district, the training is condensed into a single day, starting and ending on the thirty-fifth day prior to the poll, noted as P minus 35. Similarly, at the Police Station Level, the training occurs on the thirtieth day before the poll, starting and ending on P minus 30, ensuring that local station house officers and their teams are briefed closer to the event. General Police Training follows the same schedule, commencing and concluding on P minus 30. For Sector level training, which coordinates multiple police stations, the window opens thirty-six days before the poll and closes thirty-five days before, allowing for a one-day synchronization meeting. Regarding the physical setup for forces, the Infrastructure for CAPF camps, which must be prepared by the local Police, has a longer lead time, starting fifty days before the poll and needing completion by forty-five days prior, ensuring that accommodation and operational bases are ready well before the troops arrive. Finally, the Interface with Police concerning Nomination processes, the enforcement of the Model Code of Conduct, and overall Experience sharing is integrated throughout these phases to ensure seamless cooperation between civil administration and law enforcement agencies. This intricate scheduling ensures that every layer of the security and administrative apparatus is trained, equipped, and synchronized in a timely manner to uphold the sanctity of the electoral process.

The Mon or Single Window System serves as a centralized framework for coordinating police deployment and training activities in the context of election management, with specific reference to timelines marked relative to Poll Day, commonly abbreviated as P. In this system, P represents the actual day on which voting occurs, and all preparatory activities are scheduled backward from this critical date using negative offsets such as P-45 or P-60, indicating the number of days prior to the election. According to the official Manual on Force Deployment, a structured hierarchy of training is established to ensure that every level of law enforcement personnel is adequately prepared for their roles during the electoral process. This begins with State Level Master Trainers who undergo specialized instruction at the India Institute of Democracy and Election Management, known as IIDEM, located in New Delhi, approximately four months before the poll. These master trainers are then responsible for cascading knowledge down the chain to Range Inspector Generals, Superintendents of Police, and Additional Superintendents of Police at the State Police Headquarters two months prior to the election. Further down the operational ladder, Deputy Superintendents of Police, Inspectors, Station House Officers, and Police Sub-Inspectors who will be in charge of specific units receive their training at the district level between one to two months before polling day. The broadest layer of participation includes police constables, head constables, and all other police personnel, including those from the Central Armed Police Forces who are deputed to work directly at polling stations or as part of mobile parties; their training is conducted at the sub-division, thana, police line, or specific area of deployment roughly one month before the election.

The proposed timelines for these training activities are meticulously planned to ensure readiness without disrupting regular duties. Level 1 training, which serves as the foundational module for all police personnel, is ideally delivered through e-learning platforms at their respective locations starting seventy-five days before the poll and concluding sixty days prior. Officers specifically deployed to manage operations within polling stations undergo district-level training between thirty and twenty days before the election. Sector Police Officers, who oversee larger geographical segments, along with police staff assigned to flying squads designed for rapid response and static surveillance teams tasked with continuous monitoring, all receive their instruction at the district level between sixty and forty-five days before the poll. Supervisory officers, including Deputy Superintendents, Police Inspectors, and Police Sub-Inspectors, attend dedicated supervisory training sessions at the district level from forty-five to forty days before the election, a timeline that mirrors the training schedule for general police station staff. Strategic planning begins much earlier, with the Election Cell at the Superintendent of Police office and the State-level Superintendent undergoing preparation between one hundred twenty and ninety days before the poll. The Police Nodal Officer, a key liaison role, receives specialized training at IIDEM starting six months before the election and concluding four months prior, while other nodal officers at the state level begin their orientation immediately upon appointment and complete it by sixty days before the poll. Thematic training sessions can occur at any location and time to address specific emerging issues, whereas Central Armed Police Forces personnel are trained at the state or district level immediately upon their arrival, finishing no later than twenty days before the election. Training of Trainers programs, essential for sustaining the quality of instruction, are held at IIDEM or state facilities between six and four months before the event.

Annexure A details the curriculum for Level 1 Training, which focuses on introducing statutory provisions and preventive measures essential for effective election management. This session lasts for three hours and targets all police officers involved in the electoral process. The primary learning objectives are to ensure that participants thoroughly understand the key legal statutes governing elections in India, clearly recognize their specific roles and responsibilities in maintaining law and order, and can identify and apply relevant sections of major legal codes such as the Indian Penal Code, the Representation of the People Act of 1951, and the Criminal Procedure Code to real-world election scenarios. The lesson plan begins with a welcome and introduction lasting fifteen minutes, where facilitators briefly outline the session's scope and emphasize the critical importance of the police force in safeguarding the democratic process. This is followed by a fifteen-minute overview of the entire election cycle, explaining the distinct phases of pre-poll preparations, the activities on poll day itself, and post-poll procedures, highlighting how each phase contributes to the integrity of the overall electoral exercise. The core of the training dedicates forty-five minutes to explaining statutory provisions, starting with the Representation of the People Act of 1950, specifically Section 13CC, which outlines the control and discipline mechanisms for Chief Electoral Officers, District Election Officers, and general election staff. It then moves to the Representation of the People Act of 1951, detailing Section 28A regarding the authority over election officers including Returning Officers, Presiding Officers, and police personnel. The instruction also covers ancillary laws such as the Arms Act, the Motor Vehicle Act, and the Prevention of Defacement Act, discussing how these regulations collectively ensure a secure and orderly environment.

Continuing the deep dive into legal frameworks, the next forty-five minutes are devoted to a detailed examination of specific sections within the Indian Penal Code that are particularly relevant to elections. Participants study Section 171D concerning impersonation at elections, Section 171B addressing bribery, and Section 171C which deals with undue influence exerted upon voters. The curriculum also covers Section 171G regarding false statements made in connection with an election, Section 171H on illegal payments, and Section 171I which mandates the keeping of proper election accounts. Furthermore, the training addresses public order offenses including Section 505(1)(b) related to acts against public tranquillity, Section 153A on promoting enmity between different groups, Section 153AA punishing the carrying of arms in processions, and Section 153B concerning imputations prejudicial to national integration. These legal concepts are reinforced through discussions of real-life scenarios and case studies to illustrate practical application. Another forty-five minute segment focuses on the Representation of the People Act of 1951, specifically exploring corrupt practices and conduct rules. This includes Section 123 defining corrupt practices, Section 125 on promoting enmity between classes, Section 127 regarding disturbances at election meetings, and Section 130 which prohibits canvassing in or near polling stations. The instruction covers penalties for misconduct under Section 132, the prohibition of going armed near polling stations under Section 134B, and the offense of removing ballot papers under Section 135. Significant attention is given to Section 135A regarding booth capturing, Section 126 prohibiting public meetings during the silence period, Section 126A restricting the dissemination of exit poll results, Section 135B mandating paid holidays for employees on poll day, Section 135C prohibiting the sale or distribution of liquor in the last forty-eight hours, Section 127A restricting the printing of campaign materials, and Section 128 ensuring the secrecy of voting. The final portion of the training, lasting forty-five minutes, reviews preventive measures under the Criminal Procedure Code, specifically Sections 20 through 154, which empower Executive Magistrates and police regarding arrest, detention, search, and seizure. This segment explains when and how these powers should be exercised during elections to maintain public order and prevent offenses, reinforcing the vital role of law enforcement in upholding the fairness and freedom of the electoral process.

The comprehensive training program for law enforcement personnel involved in election duties is meticulously structured to ensure the security, integrity, and smooth conduct of the democratic process. The curriculum begins with a focused segment dedicated to poll day scenarios and their practical application, designed to last approximately thirty minutes. During this critical phase, instructors present a diverse array of potential situations that may arise on the day of voting, ranging from complex crowd control challenges and unexpected security breaches to various emergency incidents. The primary objective is not merely to list these possibilities but to engage participants in a deep analysis of the appropriate responses and specific actions that police officers must undertake in each distinct scenario. This interactive discussion allows officers to mentally rehearse their duties, ensuring that when faced with real-world pressure, their reactions are swift, lawful, and effective. Following this, the session transitions into case studies and further practical application, also allocated thirty minutes. Here, real or hypothetical case studies are presented to the participants, challenging them to apply their theoretical knowledge of statutory provisions and preventive measures to concrete situations. This method encourages active group discussions and collaborative problem-solving, fostering an environment where officers can learn from one another's perspectives and develop a unified approach to maintaining order. The module concludes with a fifteen-minute question and answer segment followed by a wrap-up, where the floor is opened for any lingering doubts to be clarified and the key takeaways from the entire session are summarized to reinforce learning. If resources permit, an additional fifteen minutes is dedicated to the distribution of reference materials, providing participants with printed or digital guides for further study and future reference, ensuring they have access to authoritative information long after the training ends.

Annexure B outlines a specialized three-hour training module specifically designed for the static team responsible for polling station security and the enforcement of the code of conduct. The target audience for this intensive session comprises police personnel who are directly posted at polling stations, placing them on the front lines of electoral integrity. By the conclusion of this session, participants are expected to achieve several critical learning objectives. First, they must thoroughly understand their specific roles in safeguarding the security and integrity of the polling station, recognizing that their presence is a deterrent to malpractice and a reassurance to voters. Second, they must comprehend the detailed tasks associated with enforcing the code of conduct at polling booths, strictly adhering to the norms established by the Election Commission of India, which serves as the supreme regulatory body for elections in the country. Third, officers need to become familiar with the strategic concepts of area domination and deployment, which involve positioning forces effectively to maintain control over the surrounding environment. Finally, they must know exactly how to respond to incidents and emergencies that may occur during the polling hours, ensuring minimal disruption to the voting process. The lesson plan commences with a ten-minute welcome and introduction, where the facilitator greets the participants, outlines the session's objectives, and highlights the profound importance of their role in preserving the sanctity of the electoral process.

The core of the static team training involves a twenty-minute deep dive into the enforcement of the code of conduct. Instructors discuss why strict adherence to these rules is vital for a free and fair election, breaking down key tasks into actionable items. These tasks include ensuring that party booths, which are temporary setups used by political parties to monitor the voting process, are maintained at a minimum prescribed distance from the polling station to prevent intimidation of voters. Officers must also regulate the number of chairs and tables at these booths to avoid obstruction and prevent the creation of unauthorized gathering spots. Preventing the display of posters and managing crowding are essential to maintain a neutral atmosphere within the designated silence zone. Furthermore, personnel are trained in managing the queue of voters to ensure orderly entry and exit, while also monitoring the conduct of polling agents to ensure they do not interfere with the voting process. Specific restrictions are placed on certain individuals becoming polling agents to prevent conflicts of interest, and the secure handling of electoral lists is emphasized to protect voter privacy. For sensitive booths located in areas prone to violence or irregularities, the training covers the necessity of rooftop armed duties to provide overwatch and deter sniping or projectile attacks. Additionally, voter frisking procedures are explained in detail to prevent the smuggling of weapons or prohibited items into the polling area, balancing security needs with the dignity of the voters.

To translate theory into practice, the training incorporates two major role-play exercises. The first, lasting fifteen minutes, focuses on managing queues and crowd control. Participants are divided into small groups and given specific scenarios related to overcrowding or unruly behavior at a polling station. They must act out these scenarios, applying the principles of code of conduct enforcement they have just learned, thereby gaining muscle memory for de-escalation techniques and crowd management strategies. The second exercise is a more extensive thirty-minute mock polling booth operation. In this simulation, participants take turns assuming the roles of polling officers, voters, and security personnel. They simulate the entire lifecycle of the voting process, including the verification of voter identification cards, the issuance of ballots, and the maintenance of order within the confined space of the polling booth. This immersive experience emphasizes the critical importance of adhering to code of conduct norms even under simulated stress. Following the exercises, a twenty-minute session on deployment discusses the necessity for personnel to know the precise locations of essential facilities and resources, such as water sources, medical aid stations, and communication hubs. The instruction stresses the need for prompt and clear communication channels should any trouble arise, ensuring that backup can be summoned immediately.

A crucial fifteen-minute segment is dedicated to the restrictions on the movement of vehicles, a common flashpoint during elections. Instructors discuss the guidelines issued by the Election Commission regarding vehicle movement within the vicinity of polling stations. They explain the strict criteria for allowing vehicles to operate, which includes possessing valid permits and carrying only authorized passengers such as election officials or persons with disabilities. A significant point of emphasis is the requirement for displaying photographs of authorized personnel on permitted vehicles, a measure designed to prevent impersonation and unauthorized transport of voters, which could lead to booth capturing or coercion. The module then moves to a fifteen-minute session on case scenarios and practical application, where hypothetical situations related to polling station security are presented. Participants are encouraged to apply their accumulated knowledge to make split-second decisions in these simulated crises, reinforcing their ability to think critically under pressure. The session concludes with a final fifteen-minute question and answer period and wrap-up, where key takeaways are summarized, and the overarching importance of the officers' role in ensuring a fair, peaceful, and credible election is reiterated.

Annexure C details a two-hour training program specifically tailored for police personnel assigned to sector mobile duties during elections. These mobile teams serve as the rapid response force and the logistical backbone for the election machinery in their designated sectors. The learning objectives for this group are distinct from the static team, focusing on mobility, coordination, and proactive intelligence gathering. By the end of this session, participants should clearly understand their roles and responsibilities as part of a sector mobile unit. They must familiarize themselves with key duties such as escorting sensitive polling materials like electronic voting machines and ballot papers, transporting election personnel safely to and from booths, and maintaining a visible security presence across a wide geographic area. A significant portion of the training is dedicated to the concept of vulnerability mapping, which involves identifying areas that are historically prone to electoral malpractice, violence, or logistical bottlenecks. Officers learn to effectively communicate with local authorities and gather advance information to facilitate better route planning and faster response times to emerging threats. They are also trained to develop the skills necessary to respond swiftly to trouble spots, neutralize threats, and report incidents accurately and promptly to the controlling police station. Furthermore, the curriculum highlights the importance of maintaining seamless communication and coordination with other mobile teams, including those from the Central Armed Police Forces, to ensure a unified and overwhelming security posture.

The lesson plan for the mobile duties training begins with a ten-minute welcome and introduction, where the facilitator welcomes the participants and clearly introduces the session's objectives. Special emphasis is placed on the crucial role that mobile teams play in ensuring the smooth conduct of elections, acting as the eyes and ears of the administration in remote or volatile areas. The subsequent fifteen-minute segment delves into the specific roles and responsibilities of these mobile teams. The discussion covers their active participation in vulnerability mapping and the identification of critical booths, often conducted in collaboration with revenue sector officials who possess detailed local knowledge of the terrain and demographics. The instructors outline the various responsibilities of the mobile teams, which extend beyond mere patrolling. These duties include the secure escort of polling materials to prevent theft or tampering, the safe transportation of polling personnel who may face threats, and the continuous gathering of advance information regarding potential disturbances. By integrating intelligence-led policing with physical presence, these mobile units ensure that the election process remains uninterrupted and that any attempt to subvert the democratic process is met with immediate and decisive action. Through this rigorous training, the police force is transformed into a highly coordinated instrument capable of safeguarding the fundamental right of citizens to vote in a free and fair environment.

The training module begins with a comprehensive role-play exercise dedicated to route familiarization and the systematic gathering of critical information, designed to occupy a thirty-minute session where participants are divided into small working groups. Each group is assigned a simulated geographical route and a specific operational scenario that mimics real-world election conditions. The primary objective for these participants is to physically act out the entire process of becoming intimately familiar with their assigned routes while simultaneously collecting advance intelligence necessary for secure operations. This information gathering phase requires the teams to identify and record the precise number of polling stations located within their jurisdiction, as these are the fundamental units where voting takes place. Furthermore, the groups must calculate and verify the exact distances between each polling station and the nearest police station, a metric crucial for determining response times in the event of an emergency. The exercise also mandates the identification of all check posts, which serve as security filters and monitoring points along the transit corridors. Participants must also map out sensitive locations as defined by the code of conduct, which refers to areas historically prone to electoral malpractice, violence, or intimidation, requiring heightened vigilance. Additionally, the teams must track and log any planned movements of Very Important Persons, or VIPs, such as political candidates or high-ranking officials, as their presence often alters traffic patterns and security requirements. Following this, the training transitions into a fifteen-minute segment focused on the necessity of swift movement and the strategic concentration of forces on potential trouble spots. Instructors lead a discussion emphasizing that on Election Day, time is a critical resource, and mobile teams must be capable of rapid deployment to diffuse tensions before they escalate. The core message reinforces the importance of proactive issue resolution rather than reactive damage control, ensuring that security personnel anticipate problems rather than merely responding to them after they occur.

The curriculum continues with a third role-play exercise centered on communication and coordination, allocated another thirty minutes of intensive practice. In this segment, participants are presented with complex scenarios that explicitly require seamless communication and synchronized action among various mobile teams operating in different sectors. The goal is for the trainees to demonstrate proficiency in using specialized communication devices, such as wireless radios or encrypted mobile networks, to maintain a continuous flow of information. Effective coordination is highlighted as the backbone of successful election security, ensuring that no team operates in isolation and that all units share a common operational picture. Subsequently, a fifteen-minute session is dedicated to the escort of polling personnel and Electronic Voting Machines, commonly known as EVMs. This portion of the training underscores the critical responsibility of mobile teams in providing armed or secured escorts to the officials who manage the polling booths, ensuring their safety from harassment or attack. Equally important is the mandate to guarantee the safe return of the EVMs after the polling process concludes, as these machines contain the actual votes cast by the electorate and must be protected from tampering, theft, or destruction throughout their journey back to strongrooms. A practical demonstration is conducted to show the correct procedures for the movement of these machines and the specific roles of the officers involved in the convoy, detailing formation strategies and contingency plans for vehicle breakdowns or ambushes.

To synthesize all learned skills, a mock scenario simulation is established for a twenty-minute duration, creating a realistic environment that simulates a full day of elections replete with various unforeseen challenges. These challenges may include sudden outbreaks of violence, equipment failures, misinformation campaigns, or logistical bottlenecks. Participants are required to collectively respond to this dynamic scenario, applying the knowledge regarding route familiarity, rapid deployment, communication protocols, and escort duties acquired during the earlier segments of the training. This immersive experience tests their ability to think critically under pressure and work as a cohesive unit. The training program concludes with a ten-minute wrap-up session where instructors summarize the key takeaways, reinforcing the vital role that mobile teams play in maintaining the integrity and security of the election process. The evaluation of the participants is not based on written tests but rather on their active engagement in discussions, their demonstrated ability to apply theoretical knowledge to the role-play scenarios, and the effectiveness of their responses during the mock simulation. Assessors closely observe practical performance and strict adherence to established election protocols to determine competency.

Parallel to the operational training, specific administrative procedures are outlined for the management of postal ballots and the issuance of Ethical Duty Certificates, often referred to as EDC, for police personnel deployed on election duty. A detailed deployment plan must be charted out well in advance for all police officials to determine whether they qualify for an EDC or a postal ballot. This distinction depends on their posting location relative to their voter registration; if an officer is deployed within the same constituency where they are registered as an elector, they may be issued an EDC to vote at a designated facility, whereas those posted outside their home constituency must be issued a postal ballot to vote remotely. To facilitate this, the Superintendent of Police or another competent authority is responsible for preparing a comprehensive database of all police personnel, including members of the home guard if they are also deployed on election duty, within the district. This database creation must occur well before the election dates and be prepared in close coordination with the Returning Officer of the relevant constituency, who is the official responsible for conducting the election in that area.

The database maintained by the police leadership must contain specific and rigorous information to ensure accuracy and eligibility. It must list the number and name of the Assembly Constituency, which is the specific electoral district the officer belongs to. It must also include the Part Number and the Serial Number of the individual as they appear in the official electoral roll, serving as their unique identifier in the voter list. The full name and rank of the police official are required for identification purposes, along with their EPIC number, which stands for Electors Photo Identity Card number, a unique ten-digit alphanumeric code assigned to every registered voter in India. Furthermore, the database must specify the location and address of the facilitation centers established for postal balloting, ensuring officers know where to go to cast their votes. Finally, the record must include information about all mandatory training sessions the person is scheduled to attend, ensuring that administrative processing does not conflict with operational readiness. To streamline the distribution of necessary paperwork, a Nodal Officer is appointed to coordinate all activities related to the handing out of forms. Specifically, this officer distributes Form 12 for those applying for a Postal Ballot and Form 12A for those applying for an Ethical Duty Certificate. These forms enable the police personnel to formally apply for the alternative voting method appropriate to their deployment status.

Strict protocols govern the completion and submission of these applications to prevent fraud and ensure timely processing. It is mandatory that all duly filled-in applications are personally signed by the police personnel themselves, verifying their intent and accuracy of information. These signed documents are then routed to the concerned Returning Officer through the chain of command, specifically via the Nodal Officer or the Superintendent of Police. There are rigid time limits for these submissions to allow sufficient time for the election machinery to update records. Requests for the issuance of an Ethical Duty Certificate must reach the Returning Officer at least four days before the actual date of the poll. In contrast, requests for a Postal Ballot must be submitted at least seven days prior to the polling date. These lead times are essential to allow the Returning Officer to make the necessary entries in the marked copy of the electoral roll, a master document that tracks who has voted and by what method, thereby preventing double voting. The Nodal Officer bears the responsibility of ensuring that applications in Form 12 or Form 12A, accompanied by the correct electoral roll details, are properly entered, signed by the drafted personnel, and transmitted to the Returning Officer within these specified periods.

The logistical process of issuing and collecting these forms involves several verification steps. The Nodal Officer is tasked with distributing Form 12 and Form 12A to every police personnel drafted for election duty. Once the forms are filled out and signed, they must be collected by the Nodal Officer along with supporting documentation. This includes a copy of the official deployment order, which serves as legal proof that the individual has indeed been drafted for election duty and is therefore eligible for special voting arrangements. Additionally, a photocopy of the officer's EPIC card must be attached to the application to cross-verify identity and ensure that no ineligible person is erroneously issued a Postal Ballot or certificate. These compiled packets are then handed over to the Returning Officer for final processing. While efforts should be made to collect all signed forms well before the last date for the withdrawal of candidature—a deadline after which the list of candidates becomes final—the protocol allows for flexibility. If, for any unavoidable reason, the forms for certain personnel could not be collected before this deadline, they must still be collected immediately afterward and submitted as soon as possible to ensure no eligible voter is disenfranchised.

Finally, the infrastructure for executing these votes is established through the setting up of Facilitation Centers specifically for police personnel on election duty. According to the newly inserted Rule 18A in the election regulations, a voter who is on election duty is entitled to receive their postal ballot, record their vote upon it, and return the completed ballot all within the confines of a designated facilitation center. These centers are specified in writing by the Returning Officer and are strategically located to be accessible to deployed forces without compromising their security duties. This system ensures that law enforcement officers, who are essential for maintaining order during the election, can exercise their democratic right to vote without leaving their posts or traveling long distances to their home polling stations. The entire framework, from the initial database creation to the final casting of the vote at a facilitation center, represents a meticulously planned administrative operation designed to balance the demands of election security with the fundamental rights of the personnel tasked with protecting the process.

It is hereby clarified with absolute precision that every individual classified as a voter on election duty is mandated to cast their vote exclusively at designated facilitation centres and is prohibited from utilizing any other method of voting. This restriction ensures the integrity and controlled administration of the electoral process for those who are actively working during the election period. To facilitate this requirement, the Returning Officer, who serves as the chief administrative authority responsible for the conduct of elections in a specific constituency, is obligated to establish a dedicated facilitation centre within their own office premises. This centre is specifically designed to accommodate all categories of staff engaged in election duties, including police personnel tasked with maintaining law and order, videographers responsible for recording proceedings, cleaners ensuring hygiene, and various other support staff. The operational details of these facilitation centres, including their location and operating hours, must be entered into the central database of Voters on Election Duty well in advance of the election dates. This proactive data entry is crucial because it allows the specific information regarding the assigned facilitation centre to be printed directly onto the official appointment letters issued to each voter on election duty, thereby ensuring that every worker knows exactly where they must go to exercise their franchise.

The management and execution of postal balloting at the facilitation centre located at the office of the Returning Officer are assigned to a senior official holding the rank of Group-B. In the context of government service classifications, a Group-B officer represents a mid-level executive or supervisory role with significant administrative authority. This officer will not work alone but will be assisted by three additional officials to ensure smooth operations. The timeframe for the functioning of this facilitation centre is strictly defined to occur from P-3 day to P-1 day. In electoral terminology, the letter P denotes Polling Day, which is the primary day when the general public casts their votes. Consequently, P-3 refers to the third day prior to polling day, and P-1 refers to the day immediately preceding polling day. During this specific window, the centre operates during normal government office hours. However, the regulations provide flexibility for situations where the deployment of police personnel for election duty occurs earlier than the standard P-3 timeline. In such instances, the Returning Officer is empowered to establish the facilitation centre at their office at an earlier date to accommodate the postal balloting needs of police personnel who have already been deployed, ensuring that no eligible voter is disenfranchised due to their early assignment.

A critical component of the postal voting process involves the attestation of declarations, which serves as a verification mechanism to prevent fraud. For voters on election duty, the declaration form known as Form 13A, along with the signature of the elector, must be officially attested by an officer of either Group-A or Group-B rank. A Group-A officer represents a higher seniority level than a Group-B officer, often holding top-tier executive positions. To fulfill this requirement, the Incharge of each facilitation centre, who will invariably be an officer of the requisite rank, is responsible for attesting the declaration in Form 13A. This attestation takes place only after the voter has privately marked their choice on the ballot paper and placed it inside the inner envelope. The attesting officer must base their validation on the positive identification of the voter through valid identity documents. Before appending their signature, the officer is required to perform several rigorous checks. They must verify that the serial number of the specific ballot paper issued to the voter has been correctly filled in the designated space on the declaration form. Furthermore, the officer must confirm that the elector has signed the document in their direct presence and that the voter's address is duly mentioned and accurate. Upon satisfaction of these conditions, the attesting officer must append their full signature along with the current date. Additionally, they must either handwrite their designation and office address or affix an official stamp containing this information, thereby creating a legally binding record of the verification.

The issuance of postal ballots and the manner of voting within the facilitation centre are structured to mirror the security and procedural rigor of a standard polling station. The Incharge of the facilitation centre is supported by three officials, each assigned distinct roles similar to those of polling officials stationed at regular voting booths. The first official is entrusted with the responsibility of managing the list of Voters on Election Duty, a document provided by the Returning Officer. This official's primary duty is the identification of the voter. They must verify the identity of every voter on election duty by examining their EPIC card, which stands for Electors Photo Identity Card, or any other government-issued photo identity document. Once the identity is confirmed, this official obtains the signature of the voter in a dedicated register, creating a chronological record of attendance. The second official is placed in charge of indelible ink, a special semi-permanent ink used in elections to mark the fingers of voters and prevent multiple voting. After the voter has been identified and has signed the register, this second official applies a mark of indelible ink on the forefinger of the voter's left hand. This physical mark serves as an immediate visual indicator that the individual has already participated in the voting process. The third official is responsible for the custody and distribution of postal ballots. Once the previous two steps of identification and ink application are completed, this third official issues the actual postal ballot paper to the Voter on Election Duty.

Once the voter receives the postal ballot, they proceed to cast their vote in a manner that guarantees secrecy and autonomy. The voter enters a private voting compartment, a secluded space designed to ensure that no one can observe their choices. Inside this compartment, the voter marks their preference against the name of their chosen candidate on the postal ballot paper. The regulations specify that a voter may indicate their choice by drawing a cross symbol or a tick mark next to the candidate's name. After marking their choice, the voter must carefully fold the postal ballot paper to conceal the vote and insert it into the inner cover, which is technically referred to as Form 13B. The voter then seals or pastes this inner envelope shut. Following this step, the voter must sign the declaration found in Form 13A while in the presence of the Incharge of the Facilitation Centre. It is imperative that the voter writes the serial number of the postal ballot paper in the specific space provided on the declaration form if this has not already been done. This same serial number must also be written on the inner envelope, designated as Form 13B-Cover A, which holds the marked ballot. This dual recording of the serial number creates a vital link between the ballot and the declaration for verification purposes. Once the ballot is secured inside the inner envelope and the declaration is signed, both the sealed inner envelope containing the ballot and the duly attested Form 13A are placed separately into a larger outer envelope known as Form 13C. The voter themselves is responsible for sealing this outer envelope to maintain the chain of custody. After the outer envelope is securely sealed, the voter deposits it into a dedicated drop box located within the facilitation centre, completing the voting process.

To ensure that this complex procedure is executed without error, the Election Commission has issued a directive mandating comprehensive orientation for all Voters on Election Duty. The Returning Officers and Assistant Returning Officers are required to thoroughly explain the entire procedure for casting votes through postal ballots to voters of all categories. This educational effort is essential to prevent accidental disqualification of votes due to procedural mistakes such as failing to sign a form, neglecting to write a serial number, or improperly sealing envelopes. Specifically regarding law enforcement, the Superintendent of Police, commonly abbreviated as SP, who is the head of the police force in a district, must coordinate closely with the Returning Officer and the Assistant Returning Officer. Together, they are responsible for organizing specialized orientation programmes for all police personnel being deployed on election duty. These programmes serve to familiarize the police force with the logistical arrangements, the timing of the facilitation centres, and the step-by-step mechanics of the postal ballot system, ensuring that those tasked with securing the election are also able to participate in it effectively and legally.

It must be clearly understood and explicitly stated that once a Returning Officer has issued a postal ballot to a voter who is currently assigned to election duty, the procedural options for casting that vote become strictly limited. Specifically, such a voter loses the ability to vote through any other method and is mandated to cast their ballot exclusively at a designated facilitation centre. These centres are special locations established to ensure that individuals working on election day can still exercise their democratic right without leaving their post for an extended period. There is absolutely no regulatory provision that allows for the postal ballot to be sent via standard mail services or delivered by a special messenger in the event that the voter fails to personally collect their ballot paper or is unable to appear at the facilitation centre to cast their vote. This rule ensures the integrity of the voting process for duty-bound personnel by preventing potential loopholes where ballots could be mishandled outside of secure environments, thereby maintaining the sanctity of the election while accommodating the unique constraints faced by those serving as election officials or security personnel.

The conduct of free and fair elections is a massive logistical undertaking that involves a diverse array of activities coordinated by a complex election machinery. This machinery is not a single entity but rather a composite body comprising officials and staff drawn from various government departments, all working in unison to execute the electoral process. An efficient, well-trained, and highly motivated election machinery is recognized as an integral and indispensable component of comprehensive election planning. The mobilization of human resources and the actual execution of polling duties represent a daunting task and pose a major challenge for both the police forces and the district administration. Given the high-stakes nature of elections, which often involve large crowds, sensitive political environments, and strict timelines, the stress levels on security forces can be exceptionally high. To mitigate these pressures, pro-active intervention by the district police and the district administration is essential. Through various strategic initiatives designed to raise the morale of the forces, authorities can significantly reduce stress levels and ensure that security personnel remain effective and focused throughout the election period. These efforts fall under the broad category of welfare measures, which are systematically classified into several distinct tiers based on the authority responsible for their implementation. These categories include welfare measures to be provided by the Director General of Police office for the entire force, measures provided by the Commissioner of Police or Superintendent of Police to the district-level force, measures provided by the District Election Officer specifically to police personnel who are part of polling parties, Flying Squads, or Static Surveillance Teams, and finally, welfare measures that must be extended to personnel from the Central Armed Police Forces who are deployed from outside the state.

Regarding the specific welfare measures to be administered by the Director General office for the force, several critical components have been identified to support personnel during election duties, including the provision of Closed User Group communication services, coverage of medical expenses, and ex-gratia financial compensation. The concept of ex-gratia payment refers to a sum of money given as a gesture of goodwill or compensation for injury or death, without admitting legal liability, yet it serves as a crucial safety net for families of those who suffer casualties while serving the public. In the unfortunate event of any untoward incident resulting in injury or death of polling personnel, which includes all categories of security personnel, the Chief Electoral Officer and the District Electoral Officer bear the responsibility of ensuring that such cases regarding ex-gratia claims are disposed of within a stipulated time period. This processing must adhere strictly to the detailed provisions and instructions laid down by the Election Commission. Furthermore, the Chief Electoral Officer, in consultation with the State Government, is tasked with reviewing the existing norms of ex-gratia compensation adopted or proposed for casualties occurring on election duty to ensure they remain adequate and reflective of current needs. This review mechanism ensures that the financial support provided to the families of fallen or injured heroes is timely and sufficient, acknowledging the supreme sacrifice made in the line of duty.

Communication serves as the backbone of smooth election operations, particularly given that police deployment during elections leads to significant movement of manpower across vast geographical areas. Recognizing that reliable communication is a major requirement for the coordination and safety of these moving units, the Director General office, in collaboration with the Chief Electoral Officer, may devise mechanisms to provide dedicated mobile phone number SIM cards to officers deputed on election duty. These are often facilitated through Closed User Group schemes, which allow for free or prioritized communication among a specific set of users, ensuring that command chains remain intact and information flows seamlessly without being hindered by network congestion or personal financial constraints. Alongside communication, the physical well-being of the force is paramount, necessitating robust medical aid protocols. Medical aid should be universally provided to all police officers deputed on election duty, regardless of their rank or specific assignment. This support system often takes the form of extending coverage under various existing medical insurance schemes to encompass all police officers engaged in election duties. It is imperative that this coverage is inclusive, extending not only to regular police forces but also to Home Guards and members of the Civil or Armed Police Force who may be deployed from other states to assist in the election process. In scenarios where immediate medical treatment from an empanelled hospital is not available, a system for the reimbursement of medical expenses must be activated. This ensures that any medical expenses borne by the personnel or their families during the conduct of the election are fully compensated. To oversee this complex process, a specific officer within the Director General of Police office should be appointed as a Nodal Officer, serving as the central point of contact and accountability for all medical reimbursement matters.

Emergency medical preparedness requires a higher level of strategic planning and resource allocation. The Chief Electoral Officer and the State Police Nodal Officer must jointly ensure the availability of air ambulances positioned at strategic locations, determined based on local geographical requirements and risk assessments. These air ambulances serve as a critical lifeline for rapid evacuation in remote or difficult terrains where ground transport may be too slow. Accompanying this infrastructure must be a well-publicized, internally distributed Standard Operating Procedure that details the exact steps for evacuation and the cascade of medical facilities available from the point of injury to definitive care. In this regard, the welfare nodal officer is required to establish liaison with local hospitals and secure stocks of emergency medicines prior to the day of the poll, ensuring that no time is lost in securing supplies during a crisis. The emergency contact numbers of these allied agencies and hospitals must be readily available in both the election control room and the police control room to facilitate instant coordination. Additionally, Chief Electoral Officers shall plan or establish tie-ups for cashless medical treatment facilities in coordination with State or Union Territory Health Departments. These arrangements should be made with state-of-the-art hospitals to guarantee that all personnel deployed on election duty, including members of the Central Armed Police Forces who get injured or fall sick while on duty, receive immediate and high-quality care without worrying about upfront payments or financial formalities.

Operational sustainability during the intense period of elections also depends heavily on managing the human element of fatigue and burnout. It must be ensured that clear guidelines regarding working hours and mandatory rest periods are established and strictly enforced to prevent officer fatigue, a condition that can severely compromise their effectiveness and decision-making capabilities. A rotation system should be established to ensure that police officers are not overworked during the election periods, allowing for the equitable distribution of labor and adequate recovery time. Adequate rest periods must be scheduled proactively rather than reactively, serving as a preventive measure against physical exhaustion and mental burnout, which are common risks during the round-the-clock nature of election security operations. By institutionalizing these rest protocols, the administration acknowledges the physiological limits of its workforce and takes concrete steps to maintain high performance levels throughout the duration of the electoral exercise.

Financial recognition for the extra efforts undertaken by security personnel is another pillar of the welfare framework. The Sector Police Officer, who plays a pivotal role in managing security at the grassroots level, is paid an honorarium for performing election duty. The standard amount for this service is set at Rs. 7500 for full-time election duty, or on a pro-rata basis proportional to the actual duration of the duty performed if the assignment is for a shorter period. Similarly, members of the Flying Squads and Static Surveillance Teams are provided a minimum rate of remuneration to acknowledge their specialized and often high-risk roles in monitoring and securing the election process. Beyond direct monetary compensation, logistical support in the form of packed lunch and or light refreshments is provided for all polling personnel. This includes police personnel, members of mobile parties, Home Guards, and others deployed at all polling stations and counting centres for election-related work. Ensuring that these individuals have access to food and hydration is a basic yet vital welfare measure that sustains their energy levels during long shifts. Furthermore, persons deployed for election-related duties are entitled to Travel Allowance and Daily Allowance, commonly referred to as TA/DA, which covers their transportation costs and daily incidental expenses incurred while away from their usual place of posting.

Finally, the psychological impact of recognition cannot be overstated, and thus, mechanisms for acknowledging and appreciating the efforts of police officers are essential. Authorities should actively acknowledge and appreciate the dedication of police officers through public recognition, formal awards, or ceremonial events held after the conclusion of the elections. Institutional mechanisms should be initiated to formally recognize good work done in elections not only by the police department but also by its sister organizations like the Home Guard and the Central Armed Police Forces. Such recognition serves to validate the hard work and sacrifices made by these individuals, fostering a sense of pride and belonging. By creating a culture of appreciation, the administration reinforces positive behavior and encourages continued excellence in future assignments. This holistic approach to welfare, encompassing financial compensation, medical security, operational rest, communication support, and emotional recognition, creates a robust support system that enables the election machinery to function smoothly and effectively, ultimately safeguarding the democratic process.

A robust feedback mechanism must be established within the electoral administration framework to ensure continuous improvement in operational procedures. This system is designed to actively collect, analyze, and implement suggestions and complaints from police personnel and various other agencies involved in the election process. By creating a structured channel for this two-way communication, authorities can identify logistical bottlenecks, address grievances promptly, and refine strategies for future elections, thereby enhancing overall efficiency and morale. Regarding welfare measures provided by the Commissioner of Police or Superintendent of Police to the district force, a specific hierarchical structure is mandated to oversee the well-being of polling personnel. A Senior Officer shall be formally nominated by the Superintendent of Police to coordinate and supervise all welfare initiatives related to the police staff deployed for elections. In parallel, a separate Senior Officer shall be nominated by the District Election Officer to manage similar welfare measures for the broader polling personnel. It is critically important that these two nodal officers maintain seamless coordination to prevent administrative gaps and ensure that no segment of the workforce is overlooked. Following this model, the Returning Officer must also nominate a dedicated Nodal Officer for the same purpose within their jurisdiction. Furthermore, the Sub-Divisional Police Officer is required to nominate at least one officer holding a rank not lower than that of an Assistant Sub-Inspector. This individual will serve as the primary liaison with the constituency's nodal officer, ensuring that comprehensive welfare measures are effectively undertaken for police officers, constables, and other armed forces members deployed on election duty. All details concerning these nominated Nodal Officers, including their names and designations, must be meticulously documented in the District Election Management Plan as well as the Police Force Deployment and Welfare Plan. Additionally, their contact numbers must be widely shared with all polling personnel drafted for election-related duties to ensure accessibility. These critical details shall also be prominently displayed at Training Centres, Dispatch Centres, and Receiving Centres to guarantee visibility for every staff member upon arrival.

The scope of welfare measures must be expanded to include a separate administrative head dedicated to private and auxiliary persons associated with the police force during election processes. This category encompasses a wide range of essential support staff, including drivers and maintenance staff of vehicles assigned to election duties, as well as volunteers such as members of the National Service Scheme and National Cadet Corps, runners operating in shadow areas, and other temporary aides. It is imperative that all welfare benefits, including the timely payment of remunerations, compensation for injuries or hardships, and access to basic amenities, are extended equally to these individuals who play a vital role in the smooth functioning of election logistics. Regarding arrangements for accommodation, the dynamic nature of election conduct often necessitates the rapid movement of forces or the temporary transfer of police officers from one location to another. While such movements are typically confined to the election period, they present a temporary yet significant challenge regarding housing, food, and logistics. To address this, the Superintendent of Police or Commissioner of Police must proactively create necessary infrastructure, such as temporary guest houses or barracks, specifically designated for providing accommodation to police personnel. Great care must be taken to ensure that these temporary facilities are equipped with adequate basic amenities, including reliable electricity supply, clean drinking water, functional toilet facilities, and proper ventilation, thereby maintaining the health and dignity of the staff during their stay.

Transportation logistics require meticulous planning to ensure the efficient and humane movement of security forces. A sufficient number of vehicles must be hired or allocated to transport police teams safely and promptly between locations. Authorities should make concerted efforts to facilitate this transport by liaising with state transport corporations to secure an adequate supply of buses. Alternatively, arrangements should be made to hire private vehicles if public resources are insufficient. The movement of forces from one location to another must be strategically planned to minimize travel time and avoid unnecessary delays. Such careful planning not only saves personnel from long and tiring journeys but also ensures that the force arrives at their destination less fatigued and more alert, which is crucial for maintaining law and order. Concurrently, sufficient funds must be deployed to arrange high-quality food for policemen performing their duties. Since police personnel are deployed for various demanding tasks ranging from managing election rallies to overseeing the nomination process, it is the steadfast endeavor of the Superintendent of Police or Commissioner of Police to provide adequate food, water, and resting facilities to all deployed staff. Proper nutrition and hydration are fundamental to sustaining energy levels and ensuring that officers can perform their duties effectively throughout long shifts.

Specific arrangements must be made at centres designated for briefing and debriefing sessions to support police officers deployed for law and order bandobust, which refers to the comprehensive arrangement for maintaining public peace. The Superintendent of Police or Commissioner of Police must ensure that proper briefing sessions are conducted before deployment to clarify roles and responsibilities, followed by debriefing sessions after duties are completed to review performance and gather insights. These sessions should be conducted in venues equipped with all necessary arrangements, including accessible drinking water, clean washrooms, and a dedicated Help Desk on arrival for reporting purposes. The venue must also provide adequate vehicle parking, a public announcement system for disseminating urgent information, arrangements for food, a layout map with clear directions to navigate the facility, visible signage boards, and comfortable facilities for seating and rest. In terms of welfare measures provided by the District Election Officer to police personnel who are part of the polling party, Flying Squads, or Static Surveillance Teams, timely and clear communication is paramount. All information regarding training programmes, as well as arrangements at Dispatch and Receiving Centres, must be conveyed to the polling personnel in a proper manner and well in advance to allow for adequate preparation.

To handle unforeseen circumstances, a dedicated emergency helpline number must be provided to the Sector Officer and all polling personnel. This number must be communicated to every policeman on duty and must be actively monitored by police control rooms to ensure immediate response to any distress calls or emergencies. Health facilities constitute another critical pillar of welfare support. Separate arrangements for healthcare and first aid assistance must be made available to polling personnel at Training, Dispatch, and Receiving Centres. This includes the deployment of ambulances staffed with qualified medical and paramedical personnel to respond to any medical requirements instantly. Once polling staff and police officers are deployed to police stations, they should be seamlessly connected with the local ambulance network, often accessible via the universal emergency number 108, so that their services can be sought immediately in case of any health-related emergency. Furthermore, the enhancement of medical facilities in areas with heavy deployment should be ensured by bringing in additional doctors from other parts of the state if local resources are strained. Essential medicines must be stocked in sufficient quantities at centralized holding areas to ensure that requisite doses can be administered to everyone under the strict supervision of the medical officer of the local unit, preventing the spread of illness and ensuring quick recovery. Finally, facilities at the training centre must mirror the high standards set for other operational hubs. Proper arrangements of basic amenities, such as drinking water, washrooms, a Help Desk on arrival for reporting, vehicle parking, a public announcement system, arrangements for food, a layout map with directions, signage boards, and facilities for seating and rest, must be comprehensively established at training centres to create a conducive environment for learning and preparation.

The operational framework for election logistics mandates that Nodal Officers appointed to oversee specific locations must maintain a continuous physical presence at these sites throughout the duration of the electoral process. This requirement ensures that there is always a designated authority figure on the ground capable of making immediate decisions and addressing emerging issues without delay. Complementing this presence, Senior Officers are tasked with the responsibility of closely monitoring the facilities to ensure compliance with established protocols and to verify that all operational standards are being met. This hierarchical oversight structure creates a system of accountability where local management is supported by higher-level supervision, thereby minimizing the risk of administrative lapses. Regarding the arrangements at Dispatch and Receiving Centres, it is imperative that appropriate infrastructure be established to provide basic amenities essential for the well-being and efficiency of the staff. These amenities include access to clean drinking water, functional washrooms, adequate vehicle parking spaces, and provisions for food and refreshments. The appointment of Nodal Officers at these respective centres is critical, as they are required to physically monitor the condition and availability of these facilities under the direct supervision of senior officials. A strict protocol governs the movement of polling personnel; under no circumstances are individuals responsible for handling election materials permitted to leave the confines of the Dispatch or Receiving Centres for the purpose of fetching food, water, or using restrooms while carrying sensitive election equipment. This rule is designed to prevent any potential loss, tampering, or security breach involving election materials, ensuring that such items remain within secured zones at all times.

To facilitate smooth operations and assist the large volume of personnel moving through these centres, a dedicated Help Desk must be established at the entrance of every Dispatch and Receiving Centre. These help desks are not merely information points but must be well-equipped with sufficient manpower and necessary logistical facilities to perform multiple critical functions. Their duties include marking the attendance of polling staff to ensure accurate records of deployment, guiding individuals to their assigned polling stations and specific polling teams, and issuing official appointment orders in accordance with the third randomization process, which is a method used to assign staff to locations in a manner that prevents bias or pre-planning. Furthermore, the help desk serves as a central resource for providing any other assistance that may be required by the staff during their transit. It is mandatory for these desks to be equipped with the District Police Deployment plan, enabling the staff stationed there to provide accurate guidance to police personnel regarding their specific assignments and locations. To further enhance navigation and reduce confusion, a proper layout map with clear directions must be created and displayed prominently within the Dispatch and Receiving centre. This visual guide should explicitly indicate the locations of key areas including the Help Desk, the Learning Desk where training or briefings occur, counters organized by sector, refreshment areas, washrooms, vehicle parking zones, and the designated flow of movement for people and vehicles. This spatial organization helps in managing crowd control and ensures that the workflow remains orderly and efficient.

Supporting the visual layout, signage boards with clear and legible markings must be strategically placed at the entrance and at various points inside the centre to show directions clearly. These signs act as constant navigational aids, reducing the need for verbal inquiries and allowing staff to find their destinations independently. In addition to visual cues, an auditory communication system is essential for mass coordination. A functional public address system equipped with high-quality loudspeakers must be arranged near the Returning Officer's table to broadcast necessary instructions to the polling staff from time to time. The audio coverage of this system must be comprehensive, reaching the farthest points of the venue to ensure that no individual misses critical announcements or updates. Before the actual deployment, a thorough briefing session is conducted where election material is delivered to polling parties in a consolidated, modular, or packaged form to streamline distribution and inventory control. During these sessions, police officers on duty and those attached to the poll party must be briefed extensively about the specific roles and responsibilities of each member of the polling party. This briefing also covers vital safety precautions that need to be taken to ensure the security of Electronic Voting Machines, commonly known as EVMs, which are the primary devices used for recording votes. Understanding the sensitivity and importance of these machines is crucial for all personnel involved in their transport and operation.

The physical arrangement of desks and counters within the facility follows a systematic approach to maximize efficiency. Counters designated for the dispatch and receiving of election materials should preferably be organized sector-wise, grouping related polling stations together. Each sector counter must have adequate table and seating arrangements allocated specifically for the polling staff of each polling station, bringing all members of a team to one place with sufficient spacing to work comfortably. To facilitate easy identification, the polling station numbers should be displayed prominently on each table, allowing staff to quickly locate their assigned area. Beyond the main centres, attention must also be paid to Intermediate Strong Room Facilities, which are secure storage locations for EVMs located between the district headquarters and the polling stations. If applicable, neat and functional basic amenities such as clean drinking water, refreshments, washrooms, and reliable power connections must be made available at these intermediate locations for the use of polling teams and Sector Officers both before and after the polling process. This ensures that staff resting or transiting through these points have access to necessary comforts, maintaining their morale and physical readiness.

Transportation logistics constitute a critical component of the election management plan and must be supervised directly by a Senior Officer. The return movement of polling personnel from their assigned polling stations back to base or subsequent locations must be tracked closely to ensure everyone arrives safely. A confirmatory report regarding the safe arrival of polling personnel at their respective polling stations must be obtained through the Sector Officer, creating a verified chain of custody for human resources just as there is for election materials. It shall be strictly ensured that polling parties ride only in designated vehicles assigned to them and that these vehicles follow pre-declared routes to avoid deviations that could lead to delays or security risks. Special care and prioritized arrangements must be made for Women and Persons with Disabilities, often referred to as PWD staff, to ensure they face no difficulties in conveyance from one place to another, reflecting an inclusive approach to workforce management. Upon reaching the final destination, the conditions at the polling stations themselves must be rigorously evaluated. The existing conditions of all polling stations shall be physically verified by the District Election Officer and the Returning Officer through field officials. This verification aims to identify areas for significant improvement and to ensure that all necessary amenities prescribed under the Adequate Minimum Facilities framework are present. These amenities include tables, chairs, benches, drinking water, separate washrooms, power connections with plug points for charging electronic devices, and adequate shade to protect staff and voters from weather elements.

Accommodation logistics are equally important, particularly for staff who may need to stay overnight or for extended periods due to the distance of their assigned stations. Proper arrangements should be made for the stay of both polling staff and police staff wherever the situation requires it. During the physical verification phase, polling stations with enough internal space or adjoining rooms should be identified and earmarked for this purpose. This accommodation planning must be comprehensive, including provisions for Police Staff, Home Guards, Armed Police Forces, and Central Armed Police Forces, ensuring that law enforcement personnel supporting the election have suitable resting places. Food arrangements must be meticulously planned for all polling parties, and packed food should be prepared not only for the core polling teams but also for other associated groups such as Flying Squads, Police sector Mobiles, and other support units. This logistical task may be executed in coordination with the District Superintendent of Police to leverage local resources and expertise. In cases where providing hot meals proves difficult due to local conditions, the District Election Officer must devise proper alternative arrangements. In this regard, the cooperation of local Self-Help Groups can be considered, fostering community participation and supporting local economies while solving logistical challenges. For the farthest polling parties, categorized as P-3, dry ration can be provided as a temporary arrangement to ensure they have sustenance even in remote locations. Special care must be taken to provision food specifically for Central Armed Police Forces on deployment, recognizing their distinct operational needs. Adequate vehicles may be kept in reserve specifically for the purpose of delivering food packets to these forces deployed at various polling stations. Finally, comprehensive welfare measures must be extended to the companies of Central Armed Police Forces. It is to be ensured that facilities such as accommodation, logistic support, financial compensation, packed lunches, or payment for refreshment charges are provided promptly and adequately, acknowledging their critical role in maintaining security and order during the electoral process.
